Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The project was initiated in February 2020 
and ﬁnalized in June 2020, which includes ﬁve months of research assignments.

Entrepreneurship programs and I was involved in a FemTech startup that developed my interest in this

innovation at DTU and learn what lies behind product development. Along with my experience in DTU, 
I had the opportunity to be part of the DTU Skylab team where my interest in startups became bigger

Domesticating Technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
1
==PAGE:3==
Abstract 
Startups are among the most innovative social structures on the planet. Their speed of change and 
level of innovation is often triggered by novel technological advances, which is also often their key 
path to success. However, the path from developing a new product to assuring its adoption among 
users comes with different challenges. As a consequence, many startups with great solutions miss 
the opportunity to reach their full potential, as their product is not adopted by the target market. 
To help support startups in their journey to facilitate users to adopt their products, this thesis aims to 
develop a domestication tool startups can use to improve their product adoption process. Speciﬁcally, 
a tool that helps startups identify the relationship between their product features and the adoption 
factors for their products when entering target markets, is developed.  
Relevant literature is reviewed to establish the theoretical foundation for the development of an 
applicable tool. Furthermore, a series of six case studies from two different countries were included to 
complement the theoretical foundation with real-world examples. The literature analysis and case 
studies provided meaningful insights into the conceptualization process for the tool.  
Finally, the newly developed tool is presented and evaluated by the case studies and perspectives are 
analyzed on how the tool can be implemented further and developed in the future in the startup 
ecosystem. 
Domesticating Technology: Innovations making sense within communities

Introduction                                                                                                                                                             Chapter 1
1. Introduction 
Innovation. Technology. Startups. 
These words have been repeated at such a constant rate in recent years that they seem to have lost 
their value. This is unfortunate as startups are often considered to be among the most innovative 
agents of change in society. Almost all big companies start small, and often their growth is a result of 
a culture or innovative product that allows them to grown and increase their size. Google's search 
algorithm, Tesla’s electric vehicles, and Airbnb disrupting the hotel market. Evidently, startups have 
proven to be the front runners of disruptive innovation. 
Startups are among the most innovative social structures on the planet, their cutting edge technology 
aimlessly bounce into challenges determined to solve them (Varbanova, 2019). They ﬁnd and solve 
problems that no one else can seem to get right. These problems range from basic human needs to 
creating the perfect artiﬁcial need. The key is that there is a need that has not been fulﬁlled by existing 
products or services.  
Generally, startups aim to design superior solutions - allowing customers to access new opportunities 
through unique methods, or aim to offer similar solutions at a fraction of the price. Moreover, as ﬁrst 
world countries and private organizations seek opportunities to invest in new markets, startups have 
embraced the possibility of creating a social impact (Pilloton & Chochino, 2009). Sustainability, 
accessibility, and social value have increasingly become inﬂuential factors when developing new 
products. 
Developing products for any market is a complex task, especially when this is attempted by a small 
team, such as a startup. Often, this task ends up being based on assumptions about the user rather 
than based on ethnographic and user studies. As a result, these new products might not be adopted 
in the new market, impacting the business negatively.  
Furthermore, if initial negative perceptions and attitudes towards products are formed - for instance, 
because of a lack of understanding of the virtues of the products, this will limit its adoption, even 
though the product itself might be useful or desired. (Bagozzi, Davis, & Warshaw, 1992). Nevertheless, 
several social theories identify the process by which humans adopt new technologies (Silvestor & 
Hirsch, 2003).  
Taking these theories into account, one could argue that there is a space for a practical method or 
tool, that better allows startups to design their products so both the original values of the product, as 
well as the product perception, ﬁts into a new market. 
1.1 Relevance of the project 
Business experts and innovation specialists agree that the current technological revolution is simply 
outstanding (Prats, 2017). The general feasibility of innovation and the accessibility of capital has 
leveled the playing ﬁeld between established ﬁrms and startups. Besides, from creating value to their 
customers, startups have demonstrated to have social, economic, and technological beneﬁts 
(Barenblat, 2017).  
Technology can be the key to address the world’s biggest challenges (Brandt, 2019). From teaching 
AI how to solve problems to long term environmental goals, startups have been at the forefront of 
merging technology with social impact (Larry Fink, 2018). Innovative solutions are leveraging 
technology to reach new markets and reinventing economies across the globe (Barenblat, 2017). 
  
Taken into consideration, this project seeks to develop a domestication model, that helps startups 
identify adoption factors for their products when entering new markets. Which, when applied, will 
allow startups to improve their user’s adoption process, and thus contribute to the functional use of 
the technology.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
9
==PAGE:11==
Introduction                                                                                                                                                             Chapter 1
The relevance of this project is split into two dimensions; the theoretical approach, and the practical 
approach. Concerning the theoretical approach, the development of a new model will contribute to 
the existing knowledge of domestication processes. Even more so, this model will provide insights 
into the relationship between sociology and innovation. On the practical side, startups can improve 
their innovation models by highlighting the role of users. Furthermore, communities will be beneﬁtted 
in short terms by the new implementation of new technologies. 
1.2 Project aim, objective and research questions 
Building upon the MSc. in Design and Innovation program, this Master Thesis aims to emphasize the 
socio-technical understanding of products and systems in their social and technical context. The 
objective of this research project is to gain insights into the research and development process of 
startups to suggest a new framework that can support the planning and implementation phases of 
entrepreneurship projects. This is done by identifying counter-intuitive new business opportunities 
based on socio-cultural and technological conditions and trends. Thus, the objective of this project is 
the following: 
To develop a domestication model that can be applied in the daily activities of startups. 
1.2.1 Research questions 
This thesis is led by three main questions focused on the innovation processes of startups. The ﬁrst 
question is related to current domestication models, establishing a theoretical foundation based on 
literature analysis and empirical data. The second question is focused on the conceptualization 
process of a domestication model. And ﬁnally, the third question focuses on the creation of a new 
domestication model for startup operations.  
Q1: How are the current technology adaptation models understood? 
Theoretical foundations and daily life appear to give proof to the understanding that users can 
participate in the product developments process. Although startups have demonstrated to listen to 
their users’ needs, they have not yet understood how their mindsets determine the acceptance and 
utilization of technology in daily life. As such, this section of the thesis includes a study regarding the 
theory areas involved in the domestication process and the conceptual elements which are identiﬁed 
through case studies.  
Q2: How may a technology adaptation model be conceptualized? 
Understanding the innovation practices that startups implement is just the departing point for this 
research. Rather, it is understanding the factors involved to make users integrate new technology in 
the desired way as it was thought for. For this, the conceptual elements of the theoretical foundation 
and empirical data are compared. With these results, a conceptual version of a domestication model 
is developed. 
Q3: How may a technology adaptation model be developed in Startups? 
This research question pertains to how startups can apply a domestication model into their daily tasks 
to their development as a sustainable business. This evaluation process is accomplished primarily 
through a perceived value study with the selected cases.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities

1.4 Key Terms 
This section a glossary with general deﬁnitions of the key terms employed in this thesis, to create an 
easier understanding of the writing in this thesis 
• Startup - recently founded small business operating with a handful of people, who provide 
innovative solutions through problem-solving approaches to an existing need in society.  
• Design Principle - tools for creating reliable experiences for the user. They are considered a guide 
that details the design decisions as developing a product or service.  
• Product - item made accessible for consumer use. It is anything that can be offered in the market 
to fulﬁl a detected need of a client.  
• Service - A way of delivering value through actions by encouraging customers to accomplish a 
result without the ownership of a tangible item.  
• Value - it is considered as to how important is something for the consumer.  
• Technology - the product or service that has been developed by a Startup. These products or 
services can come from identiﬁed needs or technology research.  
• Innovation - generation and application of a new idea that provides a measurable value for all the 
stakeholders involved.  
• Technological Innovation - involvement of new items and forms in huge innovative changes of 
items and procedures in the technological ﬁeld.  
• Domestication - the process in which technologies are developed and at the same time improved 
by the users' life. 
• Adaptation - the process in which the user becomes better suited to a new solution to fulﬁlll its 
needs.  
• Methodology - a system of methods used for a speciﬁc outcome.  
• Method - a speciﬁc procedure for accomplishing something in a systematic environment. 
Framework can be used as a synonym when it is understood as an approach to understanding a 
speciﬁc problem. 
• Concept - the general notion of collected meanings associated with a particular event, object, 
situations, or behavior.

Research Methodology                                                                                                                                           Chapter 2
2. Research Methodology 
The purpose of this design research has one main objective, to enhance the practice of design by 
improving the actual processes. 
As stated in the ﬁrst chapter, this project relied on three research questions. The ﬁrst, “How are the 
current technology adaptation models understood?”. The second and third, “How may a 
technology adaptation model be conceptualized?” and “How may a technology adaptation 
model be developed in Startups?”.  
2.1 Research Paradigm 
This research looks to combine three speciﬁc areas within the domestication process literature, 
Technological Innovation, Social Shaping,  and  Startups Business. In the area of Technological 
Innovation, the understanding of developing new technologies processes and essential factors 
involved in this decisions. Related to this, the Social Shaping of Technology focuses on the 
relationship between society and technology as a mutual shaping.  Within these, the startup 
ecosystem and its adoption of models and activities as part of their daily routine in order to improve 
their success rate.  
The Technological Innovation area is based on product development which has scientiﬁc and 
technical bases. In the Social Shaping area, the aim is to include sociology and ethnography into the 
innovation processes to ensure the user plays a key role in the development of new technologies. The 
Startup Business area, analyze looks to detail how startups work and the environment they work with. 
Having a research project based on an engineering structure, social theories are considered as a 
good supplement to understand the human factor involved. This approach complemented the 
dominant understanding of the design processes. Moreover, social theories are used to understand 
the startup's organization and the social context of the activities where they operate. 
2.2 Research methodology 
To gain an in-depth analysis of the case, Design Research methodology is used as the investigation 
structure. As such, the methodology clears the path for the development of the adaptation model. 
The research clariﬁcation stage is identiﬁed in chapters 3 and 4,  with the literature analysis and case 
studies. Following this, the ﬁndings provide empirical data to support the synthesis phase in chapter 
5. After this, the conceptualization process in chapter 6 shows the development and selection of the 
proposed model. Finally, the application of the model in chapter 7 frames the evaluation of the 
potential solution in a real-life scenario. The proposed Design Research methodology can be seen in 
Figure 1. 
 
Figure 1 - Design Research methodology

Research Methodology                                                                                                                                           Chapter 2
Based on the Design Research Methodology, the general overview of this project is explained in the 
following table: 
Table 2 - Design Research Methodology Overview 
The research mainly focuses on identifying the factors that inﬂuence the innovation processes of 
startups and develops a theoretical model for the adoption of technology within communities. As a 
result of the time limitation of this project, it was not possible to perform applied research in each case 
study to determine the viability of the proposed model. To mitigate this, the model was shared with 
the case studies and experts in the industry to receive their insights and feedback.  
2.3 Research Strategy 
Several research strategies can be applied to develop a new framework. For this project, different 
sources and methods were applied to gather data and understand the research object.  
Theoretical Approaches 
Literature Review 
It is considered that a literature review in innovation methods is fundamental when the research 
projects looks to understand, conceptualise and develop a model. It is assumed that the reader has 
an existing knowledge in the design ﬁeld and has an understanding of innovation methods and 
startup ecosystems, and so, the literature review focuses mainly on speciﬁc methods of each 
literature discipline. The literature background is divided into three areas to provide a better 
understanding of the academic areas needed to implement a domestication model, this will be 
explained in detail in Chapter 3 (Figure 2). 
The ﬁrst area to cover is  Technological Innovation. In this section, the most common methods for 
innovation and product development used by startups are listed. The comparison of these methods 
aids in a deeper understanding of the innovation processes and their effects on startups. The second 
area of analysis is  Social Shaping, detailing the framework for three domestication theories. The 
literature not only provides valuable insights about the relationship between humans and technology 
but also details the main challenges to understanding it. Finally, the third section is Startup Business, 
this literature was structured from the organizational structure to an ecosystem inﬂuence power. As 
such, this approach allowed for the identiﬁcation of internal and external factors for a startup's 
operation.

Empirical Approaches 
Case Study 
To accomplish the most helpful and pertinent information for the analysis, intentional sampling is 
applied (Patton 1990). Considering this, six startups ﬁll speciﬁc four-factor criteria; impact on 
emerging markets, operation from either in or outside the context to work with, experience talking 
with customers and users, and at least two iterations of prototypes. For this purpose, half of the case 
studies are based at Denmark Technical University in Denmark, and the other half at the Monterrey 
Institute of Technology and Higher Education in Mexico. The location of the startups is deﬁned as the 
role they play in and outside the impacted context. The author promised anonymity to all interview 
partners and ensured through the replacement of their names and their positions with the name of the 
company. However, direct information regarding the selected case are displayed in table 3.

Research Methodology                                                                                                                                           Chapter 2
The strategy applied for the gathering of data was divided into three phases based on the Design 
Research methodology. The ﬁrst phase was a structured survey to obtain relevant dialogue points for 
an interview structure. The second phase was a 60 minutes of semi-structured interviews with each 
startup. Finally, a structured survey was applied in the third phase to gain insights regarding the 
perceived value of the proposed tool in the daily activities of the case studies.  
The surveys were composed of questions focusing on the innovation process and their development 
experiences. The ﬁrst survey was sent to each startup and answered by the team together. The 
quantitative results provided useful guidance as to how to best structure the semi-structured 
interview. The researcher conducted six interviews with identical guidelines. All interviews were held 
via videoconference and recorded for further analysis. The audio recordings were later transcribed in 
the electronic form to analyze them efﬁciently. The transcription of each interview can be found from 
Appendix 4 to 9.    
Data Analysis 
The data analysis was done after each round of interactions. To obtain functional data, qualitative 
approaches were considered as the most suitable research approach for this paper. Quantitative 
research was utilized to choose the direction of semi-structured interviews. Moreover, the data was 
collected at one point in time but with questions aimed to explore the undergone activities from the 
previous use of innovation models until now.  
2.4 Research Quality 
Design Research has more similarities in social studies than natural sciences. The applied research 
methodology has some imperfections as any other methodology about social studies in the 
engineering ﬁeld. It has been stated that contributions in Design Research are almost impossible to 
validate and be proven empirically, due to the number of variables that affect each study case that 
cannot be regulated (Buur 1990). However, there are ways to validate the contributions by qualitative 
means. There are four categories in which empirical data can be categorized and judged (Yin, 1994): 
• Construct validity, the degree in which concepts are understood and able to be measured 
• Internal validity, the extent to which the gathered evidence can support a cause and effect in the 
studied case. 
• External validity, the extent to which the gathered evidence can be applied outside of the context 
of the studied case. 
• Reliability, the degree in which the experiments can produce similar results in different contexts. 
2.5 Summary 
In this chapter, the research methodology and strategy are explained. As this project focuses on the 
innovation methodologies implemented by startups, the chosen methodology for this project is 
Design Research. Consequently, speciﬁc strategies and tools are applied to have an unbiased 
analysis and assured quality through the project. 
The research paradigm looks to combine two speciﬁc areas of design and research, Technological 
Innovation and Social Shaping of Technology. When combining these two areas, the focus goes into 
the relationship between society and technology as a mutual shaping. 
The research strategy looks to compare and conceptualize literature insights with empirical data 
related to the research questions following the selected methodology structure. Moreover, the strategy 
is outlined in three rounds of interviews, where two of them are structured interventions and one is a 
semi-structured interview.  
The research validity is evaluated through the categories of empirical data proposed by Yin. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities

Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
3. Literature Review 
In Chapter 1, the concept of domestication of technology is introduced and its relevance in the 
startup environment is detailed. According to Roger Silvestor, the domestication approach explains 
the relation between technologies in social contexts and structures (Davis, Bagozzi, & Warshaw, 
1989).  Thus, in order to establish a foundation upon which to develop a new tool that can help 
startups in the domestication process, this chapter will review the existing literature on 1) 
technological innovation, 2) social shaping of technology and 3) startups business. Each of the areas 
is deﬁned by three core principles. 
 
Figure 2 - Theory areas 
3.1 Theory Areas 
The ﬁrst area of approach is Technological Innovation.  In this section, three methodologies are 
detailed as a way to understand the most common processes for innovation in startups. Each 
methodology is detailed by an objective, its parts, and its process. The methodologies to analyze are 
1) Research and Development (R&D), 2) Innovation Matrix, and 3) Design and Development.  
The second area is Social Shaping, which aims to understand three social theories based on the link 
between users' behavior and technology. The theories analyzed concerning this are 1) Diffusion of 
Innovations (DOI), 2) Domestication Theory, and 3) Technology Acceptance Method (TAM). The 
theories are described with their objective, parts, and key features of their application.  
Concerning the third theory area, Startup Business, the dimensions of a startup will be identiﬁed. The 
ﬁrst dimension analyzed is the 1) Design Principles followed by startups to operate in their daily 
activities. In this section, three design principles are detailed to provide the reader with a perspective 
of the core values of Startups. As a second dimension, the 2) Business Model Canvas (BMC) is 
introduced by framing the operational activities needed to make a Startup sustain. Finally, a 
stakeholder analysis gives an overview of the 3) Startup Ecosystem and the stakeholders that take 
part in it.  
3.2 Technological Innovation 
It is widely acknowledged that all industries face challenges. Customer needs change, budgets are 
constrained, and organizations seek to keep their ideas conﬁdential (Inc, 1998). Although the 
solutions to these challenges are not clear, different tools and applications have managed to improve 
these dilemmas. Technology, for instance, represents the biggest opportunity. In essence, 
technological innovation can be fundamental to fulﬁll new customer needs (Dibner & Lemer, n.d.). 
In this section, three methods and methodologies for technological innovation will be presented as a 
way to understand the varying processes to develop new technology. The ﬁrst method is Research 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
16
==PAGE:18==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
and development (R&D), where the general journey of companies to develop a new product is 
described. The second method, Innovation Matrix, focuses on the four types of improvement on 
existing products and ﬁnally, the third method, Design and Development, describes how to build a 
product with iterative design speciﬁcations.  
3.2.1 Research and Development 
Companies tend to spend their resources on speciﬁc research to ﬁnd new opportunities to improve 
existing products or services. These activities are most commonly known as Research and 
Development (R&D). The National Science Foundation identiﬁes three main types of R&D, each one 
with a speciﬁc objective. 
• Basic Research - Research is aimed to create a better understood of a subject, building 
knowledge relating to it. This type of research is not intended to be practical or commercial, the 
results are often related to the company interests.  
• Applied Research - Applied research is more speciﬁc and directed towards an interest. This type 
of research focuses on identifying speciﬁc tools to address customers'/industries' needs or 
requirements. Applied research is always focused on commercial purposes.  
• Development - Development happens when the discoveries of an exploration are applied to the 
manufacturing process of any product. 
The R&D time is undeﬁned, it can span anywhere from months to years to ﬁnd successful outcomes. 
The manufacturing industry usually uses R&D for new product development and innovation. Even 
though each company has its R&D methodology, a basic process creates the framework, see ﬁgure 
3. 
                                           
 
Figure 3 - Research and Development Method 
The R&D framework's ﬁrst stage is to brainstorm, which is also known as Foster Ideas. At this point, 
there is an understanding and identiﬁcation of particular issues in the industry that can then be 
narrowed to core opportunity areas. After this, these ideas must be clustered into those that have a 
wider solution space. This stage is called Focus Ideas, as the need to compare the ideas with existing 
products and assess how the new ones can be developed. For the third stage, the idea has to be 
validated through market surveys and technical feasibility, this is commonly known as the Develop 
Ideas stage. When the idea has been validated, the researchers can work on prototyping to 
understand the process to take the idea into a practical product. Before launching the product, R&D 
revises the regulatory aspects and assess the criteria needed to be part of the market.  
R&D is mostly known for the following key features: 
•
Ideas can be abandoned or modiﬁed at any point in this process. 
•
Risks are prevented by previous technical feasibility and market validation. 
•
Resources are only used when there is a validated business idea 
•
Each idea is analyzed from an objective perspective on the organization's sustainability. 
•
The complete process gives high probabilities of a successful product in the market. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
17
==PAGE:19==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
3.2.2 Innovation Matrix 
Creativity is thinking of something new; innovation is the implementation of something new (Skillicorn, 
2016). That being said, organizations tend to adopt the term Innovation to their mission to reﬂect their 
core value. Moreover, innovation is based on two dimensions or the merge of both. These are either a 
new market opportunity or the improvement of a technical invention (Kylliäinen, 2019). Although it has 
been stated that innovation has different deﬁnitions, most of them share the same three 
characteristics: generation of value, application of ideas, and measurable impact. Therefore, the 
deﬁnition of innovation in this project will be the generation and application of a new idea that provides 
a measurable value for all the stakeholders involved.  
The Innovation Matrix is a visualization that incorporates the two aspects of innovation, technology 
improvement and market opportunity, see ﬁgure 4 (Kristof, n.d.). In the horizontal axis, the increase in 
technology newness is measured. This axis includes the relevance of the innovation, including the 
core value, earmarking time, and resources used in the innovation. In the vertical axis, the impact on 
the market is identiﬁed. This is determined by market share and brand awareness.  
Figure 4 - Innovation Matrix 
Based on the Innovation Matrix four types of innovation can be identiﬁed. These categories are 
Incremental innovation, Disruptive innovation, Sustaining Innovation, and Radical innovation. Each of 
these can be deﬁned as followed: 
• Incremental innovation - Most of the innovations are gradual and continuous improvements to 
existing products in the market. Incremental innovations have slight variations allowing them to be 
better than past editions.  
• Disruptive innovation - They are referred to as a new value network for a concept, product, or 
service. This can be created by positioning and an existing market or creating a new one. 
• Sustaining innovation - This type of innovation is the opposite of disruptive innovation. This 
innovation occurs in existing markets, widening the value networks by satisfying the needs of the 
current customer. 
• Radical innovation - Radical innovations aim to solve global problems with completely new 
approaches that users were not aware of or solving problems users did not even consider. This type 
of innovation causes an impact on the market and sometime in the entire economy.  
As demonstrated, innovations can be classiﬁed in various ways. The main point of categorizing the 
types of innovations is to understand the existing opportunities and how they can be applied within 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
18
==PAGE:20==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
the organizational context. Taking this into consideration, the approach each organization takes 
towards innovation should depend on the capability and strategic goals of each (Nieminen, 2020). 
The key features of the Innovation Matrix: 
• Understand the current state of the organization. 
• Prioritizes the organization's needs. 
• Provides enough data for the planning and the implementation of projects. 
• Identify the strategic goals as key activities. 
• Allows organizations to develop strategic market plans in short and long terms. 
3.2.3 Design and Development 
The design and development methodology aims to identify the potential risks in taking an idea into a 
proﬁtable solution in the market. It considers the speciﬁcation and design of the solution through 
prototyping (NIBusiness, 2000). The design issue regards the maintainability of the solution, while 
speciﬁcation focuses on the selection of materials and generation of developing blueprints (ThePD, 
2015). It is worth considering that this methodology is mostly used by external ﬁrms providing 
established organizations a new perspective of their current portfolio (Valvano & Yerraballi, 2014). 
This methodology follows a ﬁve-step approach (Figure 5), allowing the design team to collaborate with 
the client while developing the ﬁnal product Process (Konenko, 2018). The ﬁrst step is to 
communicate with the customer to discuss the important details to understand the project's purpose. 
Next, research is conducted to generate insights about the target market, competitors, and style of 
the ﬁnal product. Third, once the insights have been approved by the customer, the skeleton of the 
ﬁnal product is designed. The fourth step is to mockup the project, allowing the team to develop the 
conceptual stage of the project. Finally, when approved by the customer, the team prepares the 
resources needed after the product is launched.   
 
Figure 5 - Design and Development Process 
Software development companies have been the primary adopters of Design and Development 
(Blanchard, 2010). Moreover, web and mobile applications are considered the best type of project for 
this methodology due to its development process and the resources needed.  
The key features of Design and Development are: 
• Acquiring the capability to meet a user need thought a customer perspective. 
• Prototyping/experimentation effort. 
• Based on the vendor's design criteria. 
• Requires a skilled team to convert ideas into wireframes. 
• The design team sharpens their abilities within each task. 
3.3 Social Shaping of Technology 
Technological outcomes have various implications for society and speciﬁc social groups (Jayden, 
2009). Social shaping theorists agree that the relationship between society and technology is a mutual 
shaping. Williams and Edge mention that the concept of Social Shaping of Technology (SST) is based 
on the idea that each person's choices will affect itself and the trajectory of innovation programs 
(Williams & Edge, 1996). Some authors mention that SST has a direct effect on economic and social 
areas (Davis, F. D., Bagozzi, R. P., & Warshaw, P. R.,1989).  
In this section, the link between society and technology is presented through three social theories. 
The ﬁrst model references the rate in which new ideas are spread by the Diffusion of Innovation theory. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
19
==PAGE:21==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
After this, the Domestication theory details how artifacts interplay in cultural values. Finally, the 
Technology Acceptance Model explains the process by which social groups interact with new 
products. 
3.3.1 Diffusion of Innovations 
The adoption of a new idea or product does not occur in the same manner for all. Certainly, some 
individuals are more poised for adoption than others (Leif, 2019). Everett Rogers supports that the 
diffusion of innovation is affected by different social and cultural factors (Rogers, 2003). Rogers is 
responsible for the popularity of the Diffusion of innovations theory (DOI). The theory combines 
multiple disciplines that provide a combination of results that allow researches to understand the 
impact of a new product in any system. Further, DOI aims to explain how new ideas and technology 
are being spread by focusing on the methods, stakeholders, and indicators (Rogers, 2003).   
The key elements in DOI are innovation, adopters, communication channels, time, and social system 
(Pemberton, 1936). Innovation refers to any idea, practice, or object that seems new to the potential 
customer. Adopters are considered to be the minimal unit of analysis; in most cases, they refer to 
individuals, but companies can also be considered. Communication channels refer to the minimum 
capabilities for diffusion to happen. Further, the time differs for each individual and every customer will 
adopt an innovation at their own pace. Finally, a social system is a combination of internal and 
external factors, such as values and thoughts, mass media, and cultural perceptions.  
Researchers have found that adoption categories have a proportional relation to the market share 
(Easley, 2010). Each adopter category has different needs and shares a percentage of the target 
market, this is explained in ﬁgure 6. 
 
Figure 6 - Diffusion of innovation model 
To achieve a high market share, organizations should consider different strategies based on the 
adopter categories (Roger & Shoemaker, 1971). Although the majority of the general population tends 
to fall in the middle categories, it is still necessary to understand the characteristics of the target 
population which may fall outside of the middlemost categories. The ﬁve adopter categories and the 
best approach for each can be found in table 4. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
20
==PAGE:22==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
Table 4 - Adopter Categories  
The progression of the stages one cycles through when adopting an innovation typically begins with 
an  awareness  of the need, followed by the decision to  adopt or reject the proposition, and then 
the initial use of the innovation to validate their decision, ﬁnally the continued use of the innovation will 
assure a complete adoption process. There are main ﬁve factors that inﬂuence the adoption of any 
type of innovation that organizations should consider for their marketing strategies (Meyer & Goes, 
1988).  
•
Relative Advantage - The perception of an added value an innovation has on existing solutions. 
•
Compatibility - How compatible the new idea is to the needs of potential adopters. 
•
Complexity - the degree of difﬁculty in understanding and using the innovation compared to 
existing solutions.  
•
Triability - The opportunity to try the new idea before committing to buy the solution. 
•
Observability - The extent to which the innovation provides optimal results for the customer. 
The DOI theory can be applied to many disciplines and has been applied to a multitude of projects. 
Thus, the theory has been molded and shaped in various settings (Easley, 2010). Although each 
discipline applies the theory differently, the lack of cohesion has provoked different perspectives about 
its validity. The next table displays the different perspectives related to the Diffusion of Innovation.

3.3.2 Domestication theory 
It has been clear that users can inﬂuence the "technology in the making", as an example, they have 
the power on future design speciﬁcations (Weber, 2015). With a rising society, the gap between 
producers and users has increased. Domestication theory is the approach in which science and 
technology describe the process in which new technologies and adopted by its users (Pantzar, 1997). 
The Domestication theory aims to highlight the role of users in innovations. It is expected that the 
work done by communities and individuals creates enough inﬂuence to make innovations make sense 
within communities (Hynes, 2009). This process commits users to a long-term innovation journey 
(Williams et al. 2004). 
This process is described in four dimensions. First, new technologies are integrated into daily 
activities. After this, the user will adapt to it, as so, the environment might change. As a third 
dimension, users start to provide reviews about the technology which are considered for the following 
generation of products. Finally, technology has enough positioning to reﬂect the culture of its users 
(Lohan, 1998).  
Table 6 - Domestication Theory dimensions 
Some of the principal perspectives of the Domestication Theory rely on the involvement of humans 
and the continued evolution of needs (Habib, 1998). This perspective can be visualized using the 
following table.

3.3.2 Technology Acceptance Method 
The Technology Acceptance Model (TAM) is a simple framework that has been applied to outline the 
acceptance process between users and technology and is the most commonly applied technology 
acceptance model (Davis, 1989; Davis, Bagozzi, & Warshaw, 1989). Moreover, the purpose of this 
method is to predict the adoption rate of a new artifact in the user's life, and identify which variables 
should be considered to make it acceptable for them (Charness & Boot, 2015).   
This model focuses on two technology acceptance measures; ease of use and usefulness. Perceived 
usefulness (PU) is considered as the degree in which an individual believes the new technology will 
improve the current results. Perceived ease-of-use (PEOU) refers to the degree in which an individual 
considers the amount of effort that will be required to be used. Being a model based on behavioral 
elements, it is assumed that when an individual decides to act, they will be free to do so without any 
limitation. In reality, this is a scenario affected by the limited freedom to act (Bagozzi, Davis & Warshaw 
1992).  
In ﬁgure 7, the model shows how the actual system use is the goal where everyone can interact with 
technology; the behavioral intention to use is considered the lead factor to use it. Before this, the 
behavioral intention will be inﬂuenced by the personal attitude towards change, which is mainly based 
on a ﬁrst perception of the technology. 
 
Figure 7 - Technology Acceptance model 
Although it is not part of the model, external variables such as social inﬂuences become a powerful 
reason to determine an individual attitude. TAM provides the attitude and intention to use the 
technology, however, this might variate depending on demographic characteristics.  
This model has been both criticized and engaged by social experts. TAM tends to work with the 
concept of perception which gives a broad range of thoughts in each applied scenario. In the 
following table, the perspectives of the model are detailed. 
Table 8 - Perspectives of Technology Acceptance Model 
Positive
Negative
Several types of research have used the model and 
gathered viable empirical data for their projects 
(Adams, Nelson & Todd 1992; Davis 1989; 
Hendrickson, Massey & Cronan 1993; Segars & 
Grover 1993; Subramanian 1994; Szajna 1994)

predictive power (Chuttur 2009).

diﬀerent samples and prove consistency (Adams

development (Benbasat & Barki 2007).

3.4 Startups 
From digital initiatives backed by large investors, venture capitalists, and technology entrepreneurs, 
startups have taken on an increasingly signiﬁcant role in the innovation industry (Schwarzkopf, 2016). 
These changes are so big that the European tech landscape is expanding rapidly beyond the 
continent's established hubs (Schorge, 2019). Analytics supports this claim by showing Denmark as 
one of the ﬁve countries with the biggest increase in venture capitals followed by countries such as 
Spain, Italy, and Belgium (Dealroom, 2019). 
In this section, the startup ecosystem is presented through three dimensions. The ﬁrst outline is the 
deﬁnition and design principles of a startup. Then, the Business Model Canvas references the logic of 
the business operations by its operational pillars. And ﬁnally, the stakeholder analysis focuses on the 
startup ecosystem and the potential inﬂuence of each actor involved. 
What is a Startup? 
The term startup has been deﬁned in various ways depending on the historical context. The ﬁrst time 
the term  startup  was published, it was by Forbes who described the term with being an 
"unfashionable business of investing in the electronic data processing ﬁeld” (Forbes, 1976), 
referencing the entrance of the Digital Revolution with the adoption of digital records. Nowadays, the 
term startup has been reconsidered. Jawahar & Mclaughlin state that the term references the ﬁrst 
development phase of any business (Jawahar & Mclaughlin, 2001). Meanwhile, Stubner, Wulf, & 
Hungenberg see it as a new business trying to position themselves into existing and non-existing 
markets with the use of innovations (Stubner, Wulf, & Hungenberg, 2007).  
The most updated deﬁnition comes from startup guru Steve Blank, who states that "a startup is a 
temporary structure that is created to seek for a replicable and scalable business 
model" (Renderforest, 2019). For this project, a startup is deﬁned as a recently founded small 
business operating with a handful of people, who provide innovative solutions through problem-
solving approaches to an existing need in society.  
Journey 
Early-stage entrepreneurs have a complex, long path with the goal to fulﬁl their venture goals (Carter, 
Gartner, & Reynolds, 1996). Startups usually begin with a founder or co-founders who found a way to 
fulﬁll a need in the market (Cohan, 2019). After this, they conduct a market validation study based on 
constant feedback from their users using a minimum viable product (MVP). Studies show that startup 
journeys last, on average, three years before they become a solidiﬁed enterprise (Shaun, 2016). The 
following table shows the comparison between different startup consulting ﬁrms and their perspective 
of development phases.

As shown in table 9 the three consultant ﬁrms agree that market validation activity seems to be the 
key element to a successful venture. Besides, the validation through measurement of customer 
response will create a larger opportunity for potential investors. Angular Ventures assures that 
prototype testing should be made at the ﬁrst stage. This is opposed to Linkilaw and Startup 
Commons, who mention this activity should occur at the second stage. Meanwhile, Linkilaw and 
Startup Commons identify the need to validate a market opportunity before using any material 
resources to reduce potential economic risks. Moreover, Angular Venture starts its perspective by 
prototyping testing. 
Resources in each stage are an opportunity for successful implementation (Manolova, Edelman, 
Brush, & Rotefoss, 2012). It has been widely documented that startups suffer from limited resources 
and therefore are constrained in the potential of tasks that they can achieve (Bruton & Rubanik, 2002). 
Considering this, it can be concluded that although startups seemingly have the same development 
journey, the development period for each startup differs from one to another. 
3.4.1 Design Principles  
The design process should be a key activity of any startup and should be included in the planning and 
budgeting of the business (Joshi, 2017). Designing for a startup is not a normal process compared to 
other design teams (Pinheiro, 2014). Teams are composed of small groups, and everybody takes part 
in the decision-making process. Limited resources imply that startups do not usually have a 
committed designer when needed. Moreover, the pace of progress inside most startups is based on 
speed, ﬂuency, and comprehension of how great structure triggers any business (Bruton & Rubanik, 
2002). Based on the literature, the three methodologies that startups seem to take as their design 
principles are Design Thinking, Agile, and Hyper-collaboration. 
Design thinking 
Validating ideas in the right moment with the right users is fundamental for any startup. Design 
Thinking merges the desire of the customer with the technological and economic feasibility of a 
company. Design Thinking is based on ﬁnding an issue that is worth solving; It is a systematic way to 
deal with the design process while taking care of user perceptions. One of the main characteristics of 
this model is the non-linear process, allowing interdisciplinary groups to collaborate and pivot their 
ideas until the desired outcome is found. The repeated practice of this creative process comes with 
design challenges that can be based on real-life scenarios. In other words, this simulation can be 
adjusted to different ﬁelds that need to be fulﬁlled (Inti, 2019). 
Design Thinking has been characterized by six stages with the possibility to go in a non-linear order. 
These stages are commonly known as Empathize, Deﬁne, Ideate, Prototype, and Test. The ﬁrst stage 
looks to empathize through a deep understanding of the users' needs. Moreover, it is expected that 
the designer puts themselves in the shoes of users to create feasible solutions for them. In the second 
stage, the deﬁnition of data gathered in the ﬁrst phase leads to a new perspective and identify speciﬁc

problems to be solved. The third stage looks to generate as many ideas as possible, in this phase, 
there is no space for value prejudices. Consequently, the fourth phase is translated into prototyping a 
MVP which will be the key for the last stage of testing it and receiving users' feedback for 
improvement. This model can be visualized in ﬁgure 8.  
 
Figure 8 - The basic pillars of Design Thinking, IDEO 
The key features of Design Thinking are: 
• Solution-based approach to solving complex problems. 
• Re-framing the problem in human-centric ways. 
• Allowing the team to approach the problem from different perspectives. 
• An iterative approach to keep improving the identiﬁed solution. 
• A collaborative process that provides diverse perspectives when working in a team. 
• Creation of a tangible representation that allows for better testing and therefore insights to improve. 
Agile Design 
Even though "Agile" has become a trend word, this methodology is known as a smart way to do 
business. By practicing this methodology, the main focus of all the activities of a design team is on 
delivering a high standard of the MVP (StashAway Product & Design, 2018). Startups usually employ 
small teams that can focus on these projects. They can deliver the solution and improve continuously 
with the correct feedback (Miller, 2018). The most popular Agile management methods are Scrum, 
Dynamic Systems Development Method (DSDM), Extreme Programming (XP), Feature-Driven 
Development (FDD), and Lean Development. These methods encourage the self-organization in a 
cross-functional team and the appropriation of departmental functions.  
By being a startup, micromanagement is required to complete any project. The time of internal 
projects is usually short compared to the impact of the followed tasks. As shown in ﬁgure 9, Agile 
methodology consists of a ﬁve-stage cycle. Evaluation and feedback are part of the ﬁrst stage of each 
cycle, making this outcome fundamental to optimize with the correct application of technologies. After 
this, the design is applied within the client to have their insights and evaluate the feasibility of 
implementation. Finally, the evaluation and monitoring provide data to understand the ﬁnal state. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
26
==PAGE:28==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
 
Figure 9 - Agile Methodology Model, LoginWorks 
The key features of Agile methodology are:  
• Fulﬁlling the client’s need through constant deliverables. 
• Tackling constant changes for the value proposition. 
• Improving teamwork and maintain a strategic distance from each other functions. 
• Concentrating on a shorter timescale. 
• Passing on data to remote groups . 
• Promoting feasible improvement and keeping up a consistent pace. 
Hyper-collaboration 
It is considered that a group is more intelligent as a unit than even the most brilliant individual alone 
(Kolk, 2017). As mentioned before, team members in a startup have a broad range of activities, and 
because of this, creative and innovative solutions are more feasible to come to reality. In a fast-
growing ecosystem, fast decision-making is crucial to accomplish the impossible (Bruton & Rubanik, 
2002).  
Hyper-collaboration contends that novel solutions are a product of an ecosystem rather than an 
individual (Kolk, 2017). Knowing that ecosystems are full of opportunities and simultaneously a 
competitive race, hyper-collaborations aims to ﬁnd the perfect balance to impulse an organization in 
different dimensions. Partners, technologies, and networks can add value to any startup (Shaun, 
2016). 
The hyper-collaboration model relies on ﬁve pillars that look to trigger the potential of any ecosystem: 
Vision, Strategy, Navigation, Engagement, and Fulﬁllment. The ﬁrst pillar looks to establish a clear 
vision to prevent risks when dealing with changes. After this, developing a strategy of what is needed 
and who is part of the ecosystem allows startups to focus on their resources. Recognizing this, a 
navigation system needs to monitor the best opportunities based on the previously stated vision. 
When an opportunity is detected, the use of the right techniques is instrumental to engage this need 
to achieve a win-win situation (Kolk, 2017). Finally, and probably the key to make this model work is to 
transform the company mindset into an ecosystem rather than partnerships. Figure 10 represents 
this.    
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
27
==PAGE:29==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
 
Figure 10 - The basic pillars of Effective Hyper-Collaboration (Kolk, Eagar, Boulton, & Mira, 2017) 
The most important factor in having a successful hyper-collaboration ecosystem is to encourage 
startup employees to engage in continuous learning. Teams have to be aware that knowledge comes 
from different sources, such as networking inside and outside their team environment. The 
development of these skills makes it easier for employees to have a problem-solving mindset 
(Varbanova, 2019). 
Hyper-collaboration is characterized by the following features: 
• Collaboration can happen between “non-obvious” partners. 
• There are more potential partners than what you can consider. 
• Rapid collaborations are enabled by digital technologies.  
• There are different types of collaborations, from strategic alliances to intrapreneurship agreements. 
• Agents and intentions evolve fast and everyone needs to be aware of this. 
• The culture of open source and sharing data is the base for solid collaborations. 
3.4.2 Business Model Canvas 
With the key learnings emerging from the market validation in the design process, startups can start 
working on a business model. However, the most important task is to ﬁrst create a value proposition 
that people want. The Business Model Canvas (BMC) is a strategic management tool for developing 
or building upon existing business models (Schaller, Vatananan-Thesenvitz, & Stefania, 2018). BMC 
aims to visualize the logic of the business emphasizing the value proposition, infrastructure, and 
ﬁnances to the organization and externals (Osterwalder, 2009). 
The order of BMC elements creates a clear perspective to detect potential risks or opportunities in the 
business. The Business Model Canvas identiﬁes the three major lenses of any business; desirability, 
feasibility, and viability see ﬁgure 11 (Jeffries, 2017). Desirability refers to understanding what 
motivates the customer in making a purchase decision. Feasibility aims to understand how the 
operational setup will work. Lastly, viability pertains to the ﬁnancial state and how much is ﬂowing in 
and out of the organization.  
In addition, the BMC is build up by nice separate boxes, each of them identifying a part of the value 
creation system. The nine boxes are named and explained below ﬁgure 11. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
28
==PAGE:30==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
 
Figure 11 - Business Model Canvas, Osterwalder 
• Customer Segments - Type of market satisfactory to current and future client needs 
• Value Propositions - The creation of value provided to customers compared to other businesses 
• Channels - How the value proposition is communicated to the customers 
• Customer Relationships - Type of communication with customers 
• Revenue Streams - How the company gets their customer segments to buy their product or service 
• Key Resources - The assets of the organization as to how it provides value to its customers 
• Key Activities - Activities that are key to producing the company’s value proposition 
• Key Partnerships - The network of suppliers and partners who complement each other 
• Cost structure - The cost of running a business according to a particular mode 
The key features of the Business Model Canvas are: 
• That it allows the creation and assessment of business ideas. 
• It is a visual representation of business logic and gives clarity to internal and external stakeholders. 
• It helps ﬁnd strengths, weaknesses, and potential opportunities or risks. 
• That it provides a holistic view of the idea, making it feasible to question its potential. 
• It is fast, free, and disposable. 
• Ideas can be created, validated, and ﬁxed on the same canvas. 
3.4.3 Startup Ecosystem 
Good startup ecosystems have proven to improve the innovation's cycle time, technology 
exploitation, and enhances developing new business models way faster than established corporations 
(Kolk, 2017). For this project, a stakeholder analysis was developed to provide insights regarding the 
actors in the startup Ecosystem.  
A startup's ecosystem is usually formed around innovation centers, such as universities or the 
concentration of technology development (Aalto University, 2019). This ecosystem consists of 
individuals, startups, and different types of organizations that interact as a system where the aim to 
develop new startups. The organizations in this ecosystem are usually categorized as universities, 
funding, support (such as accelerators), and big corporations. (Creative Commons Attribution 3.0, 
n.d.). Additionally, skills, money, and time are fundamental elements of a startup's ecosystem (Spigel, 
2017). Networking amongst different organizations and individuals plays a key role, the resources tend 
to be shared when this action is well implemented (Mermer, 2012). Based on ﬁgure 12, a startup 
ecosystem is divided by the elements of the ecosystem and the types of actors in it.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
29
==PAGE:31==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
 
Figure 12 - Startup Ecosystem, Creative Commons 
Startup ecosystems in different locations have varied outcomes simply due to their differences in 
culture and people. People’s behavior directly affects the ecosystem processes as it is controlled by 
them.  
The key features of a startup ecosystem are: 
• Startups have a higher probability of being successful when developing in the right ecosystem 
• The availability of capital is more present than in other ecosystems 
• The job creation is higher than in established organizations 
• Pivoting happens easier, giving startups the chance to validate ideas before launching to market 
• The competitive landscape creates healthy competition to keep innovating 
• Mentorship is easier to ﬁnd and follow 
3.5 Summary 
In this chapter, the literature related to adoption models was reviewed. The three theory areas covered 
were Technological Innovation, Social Shaping, and Startups Business. The following section 
summarizes the main ﬁndings of the review.   
Technological Innovation 
• A general deﬁnition of innovation is the introduction of a new idea. Technological Innovation refers to 
the process where the new idea is applied in the product, services, or tools, and resembles an 
addition of value to society. 
• There are three main types of R&D: Basic Research, Applied Research, and Development. The R&D 
time is undeﬁned, as it can range from a few months to years to ﬁnd successful outcomes. The 
manufacturing industry usually uses this process for new product development and innovation. 
• Innovation is divided into four categories: Incremental innovation, Disruptive innovation, Sustaining 
innovation, and Radical innovation. The main point of these categories is to understand the existing 
opportunities and how can they be applied to the organizational context. Therefore, the approach of 
innovation in each organization should depend on the capability and strategic goals of each. 
• Design and Development aim to develop a solution for the user based on the customer design 
criteria. Web and mobile applications are considered the best type of project for this methodology. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
30
==PAGE:32==
Literature Review                                                                                                                                                    Chapter 3
Social Shaping 
• Social shaping theorists agree that the relationship between society and technology is a mutual 
shaping. Social Shaping of Technology is based on the variable of each person to have choices that 
will affect itself and the trajectory of innovation programs. Technological outcomes have different 
implications for society and speciﬁc social groups. Economic and Social effects are the ﬁrst notable 
aspects after the adoption of new technology.  
• The adoption of a new idea or product does not happen in the same rhythm for everyone, this is 
referred to as the diffusion of innovation. It is a process where some individuals are more open to 
adoption than others. They are classiﬁed into ﬁve categories: Innovators, Early Adopters, Early 
Majority, Late Majority, and Laggards. All groups require different strategies to adopt new 
technologies and the time will be variable on the product and their demographic characteristics. 
• Domestication theory is the approach in which science and technology describe the process in 
which new technologies are adopted by its users. This process is described in four dimensions: 
New technologies are integrated into daily activities, Users adapt to it, Users start to give reviews, 
The technology has enough positioning. 
• TAM is the most common and applied technology acceptance model. It aims to have everyone 
interact with technology where the behavioral intention to use is considered the lead factor. This 
model focuses on two technology acceptance measures; ease of use and usefulness. Although it is 
not part of the model, external variables such as social inﬂuences become a powerful determinate of 
an individual's attitude. 
Startups 
• Startups are changing the world by creating an environment for fast-learning with a safe opportunity 
for failure in a dynamic business that moves at lightning speed. For this project, a startup is deﬁned 
as a recently founded small business operating with a handful of people, who provide innovative 
solutions through problem-solving approaches to an existing need in society.  
• The ﬁrst challenge for a startup is to prove the validity of their concept to potential lenders and 
investors. Validation can be achieved through the measurement of customer response and doing so 
will create a bigger opportunity for the involvement of potential investors. 
• In a startup, teamwork is small, and everybody takes part in the decision-making process. The pace 
of progress inside most startups is based on speed, ﬂuency, and the comprehension of how great 
structure triggers any business. Three core design principles of startups were identiﬁed: 
- Design Thinking 
- Agile 
- Hyper-collaboration 
• Business Model Canvas allows the organization and potential investors to understand the logic of 
the business emphasizing the value proposition, infrastructure, and ﬁnances. The order of these 
elements creates a better perspective to detect potential risks and investment opportunities. 
• A valuable Startup ecosystem improves the innovation cycle time, technology exploitation, and 
enhances and develops new business models at a faster pace than established corporations.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
31
==PAGE:33==

4. Case Studies 
The chapter presents an overview of each case study. Then, the following sections will address the 
case studies in detail to provide relevant information to the project. For each case study the following 
information is detailed: 
• Background of the startups 
• Description of products or services 
• Description of the business model 
• Description of internal activities 
• Innovation processes in their operation activities 
• Explanation of how it relates to domesticating technology 
4.1 Case Study A: ServedOnSalt 
This case study seeks to improve the energy storage for cooking in refugee camps. ServedOnSalt is 
formed by two engineering students, located in Copenhagen, Denmark, and works directly with a 
refugee camp in Nairobi, Kenya. 
ServedOnSalt started in the HardTech course at the Technical University in Denmark (DTU) in Spring 
2019. The course is aimed at students in their masters and involves students from various academic 
ﬁelds. The course ecosystem creates a technology-based incubator were student teams are matched 
to existing technology and challenged to ﬁnd a different small-scale application and make a business 
out of it (DTU Entrepreneurship, 2020). 
The students in this course were asked to develop a small-scale application of the SaltX technology 
and encouraged to not only identify the changes in the product but to consider the potential to 
change the current consumption system. Focusing on energy storage, ServedOnSalt created a 
business model that could improve cooking conditions in refugee camps. The focus of their product is 
to target the Affordable and Clean Energy Sustainable Development Goal (SDG). To make this a 
reality, the technical feasibility of their solution relies on the exothermic reaction of salts after being 
heated increasing the temperature inside a heat battery and creating a safe cooking system (see 
ﬁgure 13). 
 
Figure 13 - ServedOnSalt functionality  
In addition to this, ServedOnSalt has created a sustainable business model that seeks to provide a 
desirable and affordable solution to communities that simultaneously maintain the sustainability of the 
company. The business model is composed of a single-use purchase of 4DKK per heat battery. When 
this battery is used, it is exchanged for a new one, creating a circular system that allows 
ServedOnSalt the rentability of their services. To make this possible, they have partnered with UNHCR 
to obtain insights about their customers and users and to validate their conceptual processes.  
Their internal activities are shared between both members of the team, allowing for the discussion of 
each decision taken. When a member of the team is not in Copenhagen, they focus on the theoretical 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
32
==PAGE:34==
Case Studies                                                                                                                                                           Chapter 4
side while the other member focuses on prototyping. Over the past year, ServedOnSalt received more 
than 300,000 DKK through grants and fundraising and has had the opportunity to visit the refugee 
camp in November 2019.  
The innovation methods that ServedOnSalt identiﬁed as part of their product development process 
are brainstorming and morphological chart. The startup is aware of other methodologies as both 
members are Design and Innovation students, but they rather follow a personalized plan than a 
method. They mentioned that the onlyÂ  formalÂ  methods they aware they had followed are the 
Double Diamond, as they are currently working in their dissertation with the startup as the study case, 
and System Thinking, which was applied for the course purpose when the project started.Â  
ServedOnSalt is considered to be a study case because it is located in a physically different location 
where they look to create an impact. Besides, ServedOnSalt provides insights about their 
development process both before and after, this provides the researcher enough material to create a 
comparison of their mindsets between development stages.  
4.2 Case Study B: Nature Boards 
Nature Boards was initiated to redesign surfboards with sustainable materials. Although they are still in 
the process of deﬁning their target market, Nature Boards that aims to create awareness by using 
affordable and eco-friendly materials for the building industry. They are located in Denmark, with a 
three-person team, they have been developing eco-friendly materials for the past two years and 
starting to reach costumers. 
Along this process both students from Design and Innovation Eng. at Denmark Technical University 
found a sustainable solution to substitute the current materials of these products. With a mix of waste 
of mushroom roots, they created a strong and resistant material that could be used in different 
industries (see ﬁgures 14 and 15).  
    
 
           Figure 14 - Nature Boards offer                             Figure 15 - Nature Boards material versions 
After three years of developing the material, Nature Boards decided to approach the building industry 
to substitute non-sustainable materials with sustainable ones. They have considered a B2B approach 
but they remain in the customer validation stage. With a team of two members and a third one 
initiating in fall 2020, they share most of the tasks and gather between three to ﬁve times per week to 
keep active communication. 
The informants mentioned that the only method they have followed is the bio-inspired design cards. 
They rather restructure everything in their plane so they can decide which is the best approach to 
keep improving their product. They believe that as customers tend to change also the product should 
be able to pivot every time is needed, and by using an established method this might not be as 
adaptable to the ﬁrst iteration they were working it.
