Case Studies                                                                                                                                                           Chapter 4
Nature Boards has been selected as a study case as a result of its impact in an existing market with 
diverse competitors. Moreover, they have approached different markets which allowed the researcher 
to identify their process of client validation and user acceptance of the technology.  
4.3 Case Study C: Ponik 
This study case specializes in innovative indoor lighting for growing plants. Ponik accomplishes this by 
using different frequency spectrums and they have even managed to create new paradigms for local 
agricultural production. Started as a research project with two members, they have worked with local 
schools in Denmark over the past year to work on their proof of concept. Although the startup has 
two main technologies, they see more business potential in the grobots industry.  
Ponik started in 2019 as a research project and began by building hydroponic farms for public 
schools so they could learn about biology and data capture. With these insights, they found two 
potential business cases. First, to distribute grobots to restaurants and retailers and second to sell 
vegetable growth in a protected environment.  
At the moment, Ponik has contacted potential customers related to the grobots proposition, making 
them a B2C business model. Although they have not formalized any contracts as a result of the 
COVID-19 pandemic, they have taken their potential customer’s needs into account regarding the 
improvement of the product.  
The startup ensured that they have not used any formal method along their journey. As they have 
focused on improving the technology, they believe the scientiﬁc process could be the outline of their 
work, but as they were mostly informal at the beginning of the startup they didn’t felt the need to 
document every stage of their process as it was very iterative. Besides, they mentioned that the 
decisions along their journey have been based on accumulated knowledge rather than following a 
structure. 
This startup was considered as a study case due to their constant contact with potential customers in 
various markets. This information allowed the researcher to understand their process of approach and 
their understanding of the needs of the users. Moreover, both members of the team prefer to work on 
development tasks, making this information more detailed and easy to follow.   
4.4 Case Study D: OS City 
This startup purpose is to improve the government’s efﬁciency and trust by reducing the struggle of 
government professionals who operate within a limited budget and plenty of tech-driven offers (OS 
City, 2019). OS City was founded in Monterrey, Mexico, and has ofﬁces in Mexico City, Argentina, and 
San Francisco. This expansion has largely taken place over the past year, growing their team to 13 
people. Moreover, their main clients are situated in Mexico and some Latin American countries.  
OS City initially began as a two-person team under the name Smart City, which outsourced data 
analytics and IT consulting for their ﬁrst six months of operations. After being selected to be in 
Singularity University Startup Accelerator, the co-founders decided to merge with their outsourcing 
partners, Data 4 and Prins Consulting, creating OS City in 2019. Using edge technology, OS City 
develops personalised digital platforms for government services. Through Artiﬁcial Intelligence and 
Blockchain, they have created a modular platform allowing the government to choose modules based 
on their priorities and improve decision making based on secured, controlled, and monitored critical 
data of their cities (see ﬁgure 16).  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
34
==PAGE:36==
Case Studies                                                                                                                                                           Chapter 4
 
Figure 16 - OS City Platform characteristics 
With a B2G business model, OS City relies on three values; Reliability, through fostering city-
innovation transfer through the integration of technological solutions that have already demonstrated a 
positive impact on a safer government modernization. Affordability, ﬂexible pricing based on modular 
technology creations and implementation costs. And Novelty, pioneering AI & Blockchain using 
government’s unique data to speed-up the next generation of smart cities and governance models.  
OS City mentions the importance of ﬁnding a good tool that provides updated information for different 
stages of startups. They believe circumstances are always changing, and as a startup, they have 
adapted, so following just one methodology or method will be the worst decision they could take. 
Thus, the only methodology they have used is Moonshots. They argue that this methodology allows 
them to keep working to a goal rather than follow a path. Besides, they mention the importance of 
keep reading and understanding different ways to solve the same problem as this will give more 
resourceful information to the startup on how to solve the problem they are addressing.  
This startups was considered a study case due to the relationship they have with their clients and 
end-users. In addition to developing new technology, this startup relies on the large-scale adoption of 
the technology by citizens to create viable data for the client, who, in this case, is the government. 
Moreover, their position in different contexts provided enough material to create a comparison 
between understanding users and clients with the same technology. 
4.5 Case Study E: Kabaan 
The ﬁfth study case covers startup Kabaan. They are a company dedicated to sustainable 
construction, by converting rubble into high-quality materials. The startup’s name comes from the 
merge between two Mayan words, care, and the world. With a ﬁve-person team, Kabaan operates in 
Mexico, Colombia, and Ecuador.  
The initial idea was conceived in November 2016, during an evening talk where both founding 
members had an idea of turning the rubble into new materials in Mexico. The members also knew 
that there were business success stories in Colombia who had similar thought processes. In March 
2017, the possibility of entering a university contest EMPRENDE UAA, in the Autonomous University 
of Aguascalientes arose. The idea was incubated, and the initial business model was developed and 
presented to a jury, obtaining ﬁrst place in the category of Social and Environmental Impact. 
Subsequently, it was incubated at the Tecnológico de Monterrey in 2017. Kabaan is currently in the 
ﬁnal stage of the National Entrepreneur Award for the Innovative Idea category and stood out among 
the 500 best socio-environmental projects in Latin America and the Caribbean at the Latin America 
Green Awards in the ﬁeld of Solid Waste. 
Kabaan proposes to mitigate, reduce, and recycle the rubble from the construction industry to avoid 
waste (Chavez, 2020). In their ﬁrst stage, base and sub-base products for streets and highways are 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
35
==PAGE:37==
Case Studies                                                                                                                                                           Chapter 4
being developed. The second stage will see that 100% of the waste will be recycled and 
subsequently re-enter the production chain.  
Although they confront different regulations in each country, Kabaan has a strong B2C business 
model. Regulations in Colombia and Ecuador demand that a recycling process for the rubble be 
present, making it easier for Kabaan to approach clients. Meanwhile, Mexico does not have such 
regulations, making the construction industry a difﬁcult target to approach. On another side, Kabaan 
has found a small window where international companies look to build with environmental 
certiﬁcations making them the best service to acquire.  
This startup mentioned that the tool they can recall is the Business Model Canvas. Nevertheless, this 
was only applied when they were part of an accelerator program. Even though this methodology 
helps them to create a concrete ﬁrst draft for a business model they felt that this perspective was 
making to only see their startup in a speciﬁc way and they kept to feel inside the box. For them is 
highly important to see what is outside the canvas and how can they pivot ideas rather than just ﬁlling 
some blank spaces. 
Kabaan was considered as a study case due to their implementation of new environmental 
techniques in the same context where they operate. With team members in each country, this 
provided enough material to create a comparison between understanding users and clients in 
different contexts with the same technology. Besides, the buying decision for Kabaan technology has 
the political variable as the main changing factor in each context.  
4.6 Case Study F: Cultivo 
Cultivo Land PBC aims to capture one billion tons of carbon dioxide through large-scale land 
restoration. Conformed by a team of ﬁve members and located in Monterrey Mexico, they have an 
impact in the US, Mexico, and the UK.  
Cultivo started by merging the vision of the co-founders to create an important change at the 
environmental level. They looked to use the DNA of a startup and the knowledge of ﬁnancial 
technology to change practice in the use of the land industry. Their mission is to unlock investment at 
scale to restore nature and generate ecosystem services. For example, capturing carbon, protecting 
biodiversity, providing fresh water, and supporting local communities (Cultivo, 2019). 
Their business model works in a B2B structure, where the sale process is the main activity to match 
both value propositions for investors and potential impact (see ﬁgure 17). At the moment, Cultivo is 
soon to ﬁnalise a national deal after a year of pursuit. Moreover, they have had an impact of 626,000 
tCO₂ sequestered and 15,000 ha land restored in Chihuahua, Mexico in past projects.  
 
Figure 17 - Cultivo clients value proposition 
As cultivo works in different countries they don’t follow any methodology but have a shared space for 
all the information each part works with. They mentioned that the difference between approaches in 
each countries doesn’t facilitate the use of a same methodology, speciﬁcally when the users have so 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities

Case Studies                                                                                                                                                           Chapter 4
much cultural differences. Besides, each team in the startups work separately and then decide to 
share their insights in a moment of the week.  
This startup was considered as a study case because it demonstrates a situation in which both 
parties needed to change their mindsets to make the process feasible.  Apart from developing new 
technology, the startup relies on the large-scale adoption of its technology by landowners and 
investors. In addition, their position in different contexts provided enough material to create a 
comparison between understanding users and clients with the same technology. 
4.7 Summary 
• A general introduction of each case was presented to provide background and draw a relation to 
domestication processes. In the following chapters, the insights of each case will provide material 
for the conceptualization of the proposed model.  
• The six case studies were selected based on four-factor criteria: impact on emerging markets, 
operate from either in or outside the context to work with, experience talking with customers and 
users, at least two iterations of prototypes. As this project aimed to understand how startups 
understand different contexts, the fourth factor was the operative location. For this purpose, half of 
the cases are located in Denmark, while the other half are located in Mexico. As such, the 
comparison between the methodologies and their impact can be made when the startup is a part of 
or an outsider to the impacted context.   
• Case study A: ServedOnSalt, challenged to use existing technology they aim to improve the energy 
storage for cooking in refugee camps by using exothermic reactions of salts to increase or decrease 
the temperature inside a heat battery and creating a safe cooking system. ServedOnSalt is formed 
by two engineering students, located in Copenhagen, Denmark, and work directly with a refugee 
camp in Nairobi, Kenya. 
• Case study B: Nature Boards, initiated as the redesign of surfboards, they create a material based 
on the waste of mushroom roots that can be adopted by different industries. They aim to create 
awareness by using affordable and eco-friendly materials. Located in Denmark, with a three-person 
team, they are still deﬁning their target market. 
• Case study C: Ponik developed a grobot with new indoor lighting specialized for growing plants, 
with different frequency spectrums for a local and protected environment. With a two-person team, 
Ponik has developed two potential business cases that aim to improve responsible consumption 
and production in the local economy. 
• Case study D: OS City transforms governments into a platform of digital services for their citizens. 
Through Artiﬁcial Intelligence and Blockchain, OS City has created a modular platform for improved 
decision making based on secured, controlled, and monitored critical data of their cities. OS City is 
located in Mexico, Argentina, and the US with a team of 13 persons. Their main clients are situated 
in Mexico with a few also scattered in the Latin American countries.  
• Case study E: Kabaan is dedicated to sustainable construction and promotes the mitigation, 
reduction, and recycling of rubble from the construction industry. The company favors nature and 
wishes to avoid its exploitation. With a ﬁve-person team, Kabaan is located and operates in Mexico, 
Colombia, and Ecuador.  
• Case study F: Cultivo by matching value propositions for investors and potential impact land, Cultivo 
aims to capture one billion tons of carbon dioxide through large-scale land restoration. Composed 
of a ﬁve-person team, this startup is located in Monterrey Mexico, and has an impact in the USA, 
Mexico, and the UK.

==PAGE:39==
Understanding the Synthesis of Domestication Models                                                                                         Chapter 5
5. Understanding the Synthesis of Domestication Models 
As a supplement to Chapter 3, this chapter seeks to fulﬁll the ﬁrst research question, How are the 
current technology adaptation models understood?, by establishing a theoretical foundation for 
the synthesis of Domestication models. Based on theoretical frameworks in the Engineering Design 
ﬁeld, the design of models and identiﬁcation of their fundamental aspects will be detailed. 
This chapter starts by outlining the properties of design models and the requirements they should 
contain in order to provide quality results. After this, the conceptual elements for the Domestication 
Models are described, identifying the fundamental parts for the development of a new model.  
5.1 Developing of theoretical frameworks 
Theories and models are used to describe empirical phenomena (Tan, 2010). As before mentioned, 
this project seeks to develop a tool for domestication models that can be applied in the daily activities 
of startups. 
To make this possible, the Design Research model is identiﬁed as the research structure for this 
project. Therefore, the approach to propose this new model is to identify the fundamental elements for 
a domestication process through literature and empirical data and correlate them. 
As this project intends to improve a startup’s potential, it has been noted through the case studies 
that the performance of its members seems to be a key factor for success. Taking this into account, 
the development of the domestication model should be based on activities that team members can 
achieve through individual or team efforts. An important requirement for this model is to consider the 
difference of resources each startup has and the viability of adopting this strategy as a new way to 
work.  
5.1.1. Design models 
Models allow researchers to study the design process at an early stage. They help in the knowledge 
of the design parameters and deﬁne the course in which the development should occur (Tan, 2010). 
Design models are a representation to communicate their larger physical characteristics and help to 
analyze and evaluate simulations of potential behavior in speciﬁc contexts (M., 1995).  
Buur and Andreasen identiﬁed that information can only exist in a medium. This medium is often a 
graphical form to send a message, for instance, human language, symbols, drafting standards, and/
or projections. Even though the information might be sent in the most optimal form, a correct 
reception might be interrupted by external factors (Buur & Myrup Andreasen, 1989). For this, they 
identiﬁed two dimensions in which design models can be understood: abstraction and detailing, see 
ﬁgure 18 (Buur & Myrup Andreasen, 1989). 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
38
==PAGE:40==
Understanding the Synthesis of Domestication Models                                                                                         Chapter 5
 
Figure 18 - Degree of abstraction - Buur & Andreasen, 1989 
Taking into account the literature review and case studies ﬁndings, it is understood that the proposed 
model should consider a level for detailed information and a medium level for abstraction, as this will 
allow the users reﬂect on their interactions with the model. 
5.2 Fundamental dimensions of Domestication Models 
In Chapter 3 the common understanding in the literature about domestication models is described. As 
mentioned by Roger Silvestor, the domestication approach explains the relation between technologies 
in social contexts and structures (Davis, Bagozzi, & Warshaw, 1989). Therefore, three theory areas 
were identiﬁed as the main dimensions of the domestication process. 1) Technological Innovation, 2) 
Social Shaping, and 3) Startup Business. 
 
Figure 20 - Meta model for Domestication Models 
The ﬁrst dimension to consider is the Technological Innovation which pertains to identifying current 
methodologies as a way to understand the most common processes for innovation within startups. In 
this dimension, the planning of activities is a crucial factor that will allow the intervention of a new 
model into established decisions. 
The second dimension is  Social Shaping. The focus of this dimension is the relationship between 
human behavior and technology. The theories presented in Chapter 2 let the researcher understand 
the established process where the focus is on customer activities.  
Finally, the third dimension, Startup Business, considers the internal and external activities that take 
place in the startup ecosystem. Incorporating the Design Principles, startups can operate in their daily 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
39
==PAGE:41==
Understanding the Synthesis of Domestication Models                                                                                         Chapter 5
activities and make their BMC a reality to the network potential. As such, understanding this 
dimension allows the researcher to understand the available resources and the potential of each 
startup.  
Combing these dimensions makes it feasible to approach the domestication process from different 
perspectives. It allows for the understanding of the economic and social impacts of the startup 
ecosystem. Moreover, these dimensions create a holistic intervention when applying methodologies 
for developing technologies, and interact with the customers and users.  
5.3 Conceptual elements of Domestication Models 
In past chapters, a broad range of literature research and ﬁeldwork on startups R&D processes is 
conducted. This included research within Technology Innovation, Social Shaping, and Startup 
Business. Further, the case studies aim to understand the history of the context, stakeholders 
involved, and the accessibility and application of new technologies developed by the startups. 
The literature in Chapter 3 is focused on exploring different models related to study areas in the 
domestication process. Technology Innovation focuses on the innovation processes and the spread of 
technology, and Social Shaping, provides insights about technology acceptance behavior. Moreover, 
Startups Business produce information for the development phases of the teams and internal 
activities. 
Case studies in chapter 4 provides empirical data about the startups domestication models and 
operations. From internal activities to a speciﬁc use of methods, insights about their interpretation of 
customer needs and technology elements were uncovered. 
Applying Hansen and Andreasen's exploration model (ﬁgure 19), the principal ﬁndings of the literature 
review and case studies are correlated to the ﬁve main feed chains and use as the beginning point for 
the concept synthesis process (Andreasen, 2015). The feed chains provide the following structure: 
•
For the need interpretation the case studies shown multiple perspectives of the research 
paradigm.  
•
In the task perception and formulation paradoxes statements based on the literature review 
and case studies are developed 
•
For the perception of preferences a sample group was selected to have multiple perspectives. 
•
The technology and ideas are based on the literature review of domestication process and 
case studies. 
•
The problem statement is based on the second research question: How may a technology 
adaptation model be conceptualized? 
 
Figure 19 - Exploration model, Hansen and Andreasen 2003

Andreasen, the following attributes are considered for the conceptualization of the domestication 
model: 
•
Business strategy - Startups rely on their value proposition to be economically sustainable.  
•
Technological  potential -  The degree in which the solution can produce similar results in 
different contexts. 
•
Customer activity focus - Value and fulﬁllment of needs can only be understood by analyzing 
customer and user behavior with and without the solution. 
•
System  Thinking  - By realizing systems are made of different factors that will affect the 
perspective of each technology.  
•
Life  cycle consideration -  Considering the production and consumption of the solution in a 
different context. 
•
Network potential - Understanding the potential of different stakeholders in different industries. 
5.4 Behavior properties of Domestication Models 
A usual characteristic of any model developed is the behavioral properties of its attributes. As 
previously mentioned, the dimensions selected for the development of this model provide a varied 
perspective, aiming to provoke a change within speciﬁcs areas. The use of any model is determined 
by their perception of the use and value of the attributes. Based upon the identiﬁcation of the 
conceptual elements of domestication models and the fundamental dimensions, an identiﬁcation of 
the important value in each relation between attributes and dimensions are presented in the following 
table 10.  
These behavioral properties are identiﬁed through the literature review and case studies. With six 
attributes and three dimensions, 24 properties were recognized as a value relation for the 
development of the domestication model.

5.5 Summary 
This chapter presents the theoretical framework in order to provide an understanding of the synthesis 
of Domestication Models. The reviewed framework provides a theoretical foundation for the ﬁrst 
research question, How are the current technology adaptation models understood?.  
The dimensions of the domestication model are identiﬁed as the main theory areas of the 
domestication process: (1) Technological Innovation (2) Social Shaping and (3) Startup Business. 
Based on the literature presented, the insights derived from the case studies, and the literature review, 
the models' attributes are selected.  
•

•

different contexts.

behavior properties in the model. The model’s base has been explained, but its applicability has yet to 
be fully validated. This process will be done in chapter 6, with the conceptualization of the model, and 
in chapter 7, with the application in the startup activities.

6. Domestication Model Conceptualization 
In this chapter, the second research question is addressed:  How may a technology adaptation 
model be conceptualized?. To answer this question, a level of coherence is necessary amongst the 
design concepts. Hansen and Andreasen (2003) mention three aspects that should be considered in 
regards to this process: 
•
Identiﬁcation of conceptual aspects 
•
Modeling of concepts 
•
Evaluation criteria 
The following sections elaborate on the development of each aspect and provides material for the 
following one. In the identiﬁcation of conceptual aspects, the-idea-with-and-idea-in for each attribute 
is listed and paradoxes of the domestication models are created based on the literature review and 
case studies. After this, in the modeling of concepts section, the main ﬁndings of the research are 
clustered into essential characteristics of the domestication model should contain. Finally, the 
evaluation criteria based on the  difference that matters and raison d’être  identiﬁes the most 
meaningful parts and coherent ways to select the concept with more potential to become the 
proposed model. 
6.1 Identiﬁcation of Conceptual Aspects 
To identify in what course this project is running, the design task is outlined. It is fundamental to 
consider what is the objective, who will use the concept, and what does it need to work out. To shape 
the task, the idea with and idea in of each attribute is detailed in the following table, resulting as a 
deeper understanding of conceptual aspects. The  idea with expresses the need aspects, and 
the idea in explains the implementation of the need (Hansen and Andreasen, 2003). This allows the 
design of paradoxes and identiﬁcation of the task formulation on the further part of this process. The 
task looks to incorporate as many of the paradoxes as is possible and coherent.

and under which conditions.  
The domestication model seeks to improve the adoption of new technologies by highlighting the 
role of users. The model will improve the understanding of users perceptions when experts 
develop solutions for a diﬀerent location to the one they are. As a result of the model, Startups will 
understand the system to work with and create a balance between the customer and the company 
needs.

The Morphology chart prompted three promising concepts. Each of them is developed and detailed

6.3 Evaluation Criteria 
Design concepts are developed ideas that have a speciﬁc degree of tangibility. Hansen and 
Andreasen (2005) discuss eight dimensions that make a concept strong; strategy, business, need, 
user and customer, technology, product, task, goal speciﬁcation (ﬁgure 21). Nonetheless, the authors 
emphasize that is not required to fulﬁl all these dimensions, rather, one should recognize and work on 
the difference that matters. 
 
Figure 21 - Product idea framework , Hansen and Andreasen 2003 
Considering the framework for a product idea, an analysis of each concept is conducted to achieve 
an objective evaluation (see appendix 12). Each concept is separated by its components and 
identiﬁed to which dimension each part impacts. Besides the product idea framework, each concept 
is also validated with the product's raison d’être (Hansen and Andreasen, 2005). Two arguments are 
developed for each of the characteristics, as a result, the concept vulnerability can be interpreted. 
Recognizing the main objective of the proposed model, concept #3 was identiﬁed as the best option.  
For concept #3 it is considered that the following dimensions in the product idea framework are 
impacted. These are described as the following: 
•
Strategy, the model will allow the startup to create a plan to achieve its mission and vision 
while having a balance between user and company. 
•
Need, this concept proposes a relationship between customers and product attributes.  
•
User, and  customer, this concept shows the relation between the adoption factors that are 
relevant for the user and customer in order to accept the product. 
•
Goal speciﬁcation, by applying this concept the properties and performance of the product are 
validated before user testing. 
•
Product,  the form and appearance of a product, as well as the technical solutions are 
correlated to the adoption factors. 
•
Technology, the product principles are outlined and detailed in technical and user perception. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities

Domestication Model Conceptualization                                                                                                                Chapter 6
 
Figure 22 - Evaluation of proposed tool 
In addition, the arguments for Product’s raison d’être are detailed as the following: 
Possible 
    + With an iterative tool is feasible to adopt it in the startups' scenarios 
    - Each startup will have a different way to use it (e.g. teams, individual, whole company) 
Profitable 
    + Reduces the workforce creating bigger opportunities to improve current solutions. 
    - It will require time to draft the ﬁrst version of the canvas, taking the time of other tasks.  
Desirable 
    + The outcomes makes it more appealing compared to current solutions. 
    - Some startups might consider it as not needed in their operations. 
Sensible 
    + The product beneﬁts the end-user and the customer by improving current solutions  
-The product has to be improved in graphical design to make it more attractive.  
6.4 Proposed tool for Domestication of Technology  
Considering the comprehensive knowledge obtained in the literature review and the case studies, the 
conceptualization process gave a promising conceptual model. In order to demonstrate the proposed 
model, a description is provided, followed by the identiﬁcation of the most crucial aspects and how 
they behave, this is supported by an illustration. Finally, the key features conclude with the description 
of the tool. 
The Mainstream Market Canvas is a strategic tool to quickly and easily deﬁne and communicate to 
which extend a technology meets the adoption process factors. The canvas is a one-page document 
that works through the fundamental stages of the adoption process and the main characteristics of 
the selling product, structuring an overview of the adoption and technology ﬁt, this can be seen in 
ﬁgure 23.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
47
==PAGE:49==
Domestication Model Conceptualization                                                                                                                Chapter 6
 
Figure 23 - Proposed Domestication Tool 
With a non-linear, iterative process, the Mainstream Market Canvas seeks to understand users, 
challenge assumptions, and create innovative solutions to test. The top portion of the tool focuses on 
the adoption factors necessary for a user to adopt a product while the left side emphasizes the 
product characteristics. The relation between each factor is considered as the value between the 
product and the adoption factors, consequently a variable for the product to be adopted into the 
user’s life.  
As a ﬁrst interaction with the tool, the four stages of the adoption process are details with their 
speciﬁc factors underneath. To provide a clearer understanding, each stage, factor, and key questions 
are deﬁned as the following:

6.6. Summary 
Related to the second research question, How may a technology adaptation model be 
conceptualised?, this thesis proposes a domestication tool to be used in the daily activities of 
startups. In response to this, three potential models are developed following the conceptualization 
process outlined by Hansen and Andreasen (see ﬁgure 24). The analysis of the literature review and 
case studies demonstrates that the fundamental dimensions detailed in Chapter 5 are necessary. 
 
Figure 24 - Conceptualisation process 
The conceptualization process is divided into three phases,  Identiﬁcation of conceptual aspects, 
modeling of concepts, and evaluation criteria. For the identiﬁcation of conceptual aspects, the Idea 
with-and-Idea-in method is applied to the attributes for domestication models. With these, it was 
possible to create paradoxes that were used to deﬁne the task for the proposed tool.  
After this, the insights from the literature review and the case studies are categorized and 
subsequently clustered into the chosen characteristics which are then arranged in a morphology 
chart. By clustering different characteristics, three conceptual models are developed.  
Finally, each of the concepts is evaluated through the product idea framework and the raison d’être 
method, which allowed for the identiﬁcation of the strongest concept. Thus, the concept chosen was 
#3 - Mainstream Market Canvas. 
Nonetheless, like any other contribution to the Design Research ﬁeld, there are always chances that 
the proposed tool will not be adopted by startups.. 
Usability 
Process where the 
individual interacts with the 
artifact.
Ease of use: 
The degree to which the user understands the amount 
of eﬀort needed to take advantage of the product. 
• What are the steps that the user has to do in order to achieve the 
expected value? 
• What are the most challenging steps?

How comfortable the user becomes using the product, 
it takes less time to use and the experience improves

Process of converting the

• What strategy should be employed to make the user keep using 
the product?
Motivation to recommend: 
The reasons the user will consider sharing their 
experience with the product. These can be directly 
related to the functionality or the experience feelings.
• What are the reasons the user will recommend the product? 
• How has the product improved the user’s life? 
• What characteristics does the user share with potential 
customers?

==PAGE:51==
Domestication Model Application                                                                                                                           Chapter 7
7. Domestication Model Application 
This chapter seeks to answer the third research question,  How may a technology adaptation 
model be developed in Startups?. This is done by establishing the proposed tool in the context of 
the design and development processes of startups. As there was a limited time period, this was 
completed by conducting a perception value questionnaire with the case studies.  
7.1 Identiﬁcation of Perceived Value 
To analyze the potential of the tool, the questionnaire aims to identify the perceived value and potential 
of the tool by the case studies. Perceived value is the client's assessment of the quality of an item 
based on their personal experiences (Kopp, 1970). The perceived value for this research project is 
constituted by action, barriers and motivation factors. These factors are analyzed through how 
positive or negative the user’s perception of the tool is.  
For each case study, an interactive board detailing the tool and a basic guideline are sent together 
with the questionnaire to the case studies. The structure of the questionnaire is as following (The 
interactive board and questionnaire can also be found in appendix 13). 
Action 
These questions strive to distinguish the emotions related to the model, and the effect they have to 
adopt the model. 
• Scale question: On a scale of 1-10, how easy was it to understand the tool? 
• Scale question: On a scale of 1-10, how comfortable will you feel using the tool? 
• Open-ended question: Which three words would you use to describe the tool? 
Barriers 
These questions focus on how users internalize and understand the tool. 
• Open-ended question: Please share a few things that can be improved in the tool. 
• Open-ended question: In your perception, what is the biggest challenge to using this tool? 
• Open-ended question: In your perception, will this tool provide meaningful information to the 
startup? 
Motivation 
These questions answer how positive or negative the user perception has been with the tool. 
• Scale question: On a scale of 1-10, how likely are you to use it in your startup? 
• Scale question: On a scale of 1-10, how likely are you to recommend to another startup or 
colleague? 
• Open-ended question: Why would you use it in your daily operations? 
• Open-ended question: Please share a few things that you liked about the tool. 
7.2 Domestication Tool related to Startup perception 
The proposed design is evaluated concerning the perceived value by the case studies. As previously 
mentioned, the questionnaire is divided into three sections to identify the level of understandability, the 
ease of use, and the perception of the model. For this evaluation, scale and open-ended questions 
are applied to provide qualitative and quantitative data. It is considered that a seven out of ten is a 
pass grade for the scale questions.  
In the ﬁrst section of the questionnaire, Action, the aim is to identify the feelings connected to the 
model. Two scale questions and one open ended question provides the ﬁndings for this section. The 
ﬁrst outcome is that ﬁve of the six case studies approves the proposed tool as easy to understand 
and use (see ﬁgure 25 and 26). The sixth case mentions it is difﬁcult to ﬁll each cell without a guide, 
leading the researcher to assume the key questions for the tool have not been noticed, as the other 
case studies make an emphasis of how this questions help them to understand the way of use. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
50
==PAGE:52==
Domestication Model Application                                                                                                                           Chapter 7
 
Figure 25 - Understand level of proposed tool 
 
Figure 26 - Comfort level of proposed tool 
In the Barriers section, the focus is on how users internalize and understand the tool. For this, ﬁve out 
of the six cases determine the proposed tool is a meaningful resource for data gathering (See ﬁgure 
27). Once again, the sixth case ﬁnds the tool irrelevant by arguing that the canvas by itself was just an 
outline of product characteristics rather than a representative visualization. Meanwhile, the other case 
studies cite a reﬂection of the outcomes and appropriate these ﬁndings into a reﬂection process while 
ﬁlling the canvas.  
The second section has a more qualitative focus so the researcher can analyze the challenges and 
opportunities of the tool perceived by the startups. The two main challenges uncovered in the results 
are the mental work that startups have to put into working all the sections of the tool at once and the 
need for a reﬂection guide to making the teams be aware of the relation of the outcomes in their 
canvas. Additionally, the identiﬁed opportunities include low complexity visuals, which allowed those 
with key questions to ﬁll the canvas and consider how it can be applied to services.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
51
==PAGE:53==
Domestication Model Application                                                                                                                           Chapter 7
 
Figure 27 - Meaningful resource answers 
Lastly, in the third section, Motivation, the aim is to analyze the user's perception of the tool. A net 
promoter score is identiﬁed along with the reasoning behind using the tool in the daily operations of 
the startup (see ﬁgure 28). The net promoter score is 34, generally, a score between 0 and 30 is a 
good indication of where the tool is situated but there is still room to improve (Retently, 2020). Thus, a 
score above 30 indicates the tool is exceptional and users are fulﬁlled.  
Reasons to use the tool included the fact that the tool creates team awareness of where are they 
standing with product development and the easy identiﬁcation of risk and opportunities before user 
validation. Further, the reasons to recommend the tool are analyzed and it is found that it relies on the 
focus in the product life when it is with the user and the visual aid making team members relate their 
tasks to product improvement.  
 
Figure 28 - Net Promoter Score 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
52
==PAGE:54==
Domestication Model Application                                                                                                                           Chapter 7
7.3 Summary 
This chapter explores the third research question, How may a domestication model be developed 
in Startups?.  To do this,  a presentation with an illustration of the tool and a basic guideline is 
distributed together with the questionnaire to the case studies, in order to to gauge their perception of 
the proposed tool. In each of the case studies, the relationship between actions, barriers, and 
motivations are analyzed. The main ﬁndings in this evaluation are the following: 
•
Five out of the six case studies indicated that the proposed tool is easy to understand and 
use. For the sixth case there is an assumption that the guideline of the tool is not noticed, as 
the other results emphasize how that this guide helped them to understand the tool. 
•
Five out of the six cases ﬁnd the proposed tool as a meaningful resource for data gathering. 
The sixth case ﬁnds the tool as irrelevant by arguing that the canvas by itself was only an 
outline of the product characteristics without any extra information. 
•
The two main challenges to using the tool are identiﬁed as mental exhaustion and the use of a 
guide for reﬂection on the outcomes.  
•
The Net Promoter Score of the tool is 34, meaning that it is doing well and has more happy 
users than unhappy ones.  
•
The reasons to keep using and recommend the tool rely on the focus in the product life when it 
is with the user and the visual aid making team members relate their tasks to product 
improvement.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
53
==PAGE:55==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
8. Discussion 
The chapter discusses the main ﬁndings concerning its validity and current knowledge in the design 
area. The research in this project focused on the conceptualization of a domestication tool through 
the design research methodology. So far, the domestication process has been investigated in relation 
to the interaction of a product when it is already owned by the user, but not applying a tool in the 
development process to improve this relationship between user-product. 
For each validity category, the research quality is approached in the same order as the research 
methodology is applied through the project. Following, the ﬁndings regarding existing knowledge in 
the ﬁeld are discussed.  
8.1 Research validity 
As stated by Yin in Chapter 3, there are four categories were empirical data can be classiﬁed to 
assess the validity of research results. The four categories are    Construct validity, Internal validity, 
External validity, and Reliability (Yin, 2009). Each of the categories is explained in the same order as 
the research was happening.  
8.1.1 Construct validity 
As mentioned by Yin (1994), to ensure construct validity, the research questions and theoretical 
foundations should be clearly deﬁned before starting any process (Chapters 1 and 2). Besides, to 
support this validity, Yin recommends using varying types of sources to create a chain of evidence 
that has different actors reviewing the draft project.  
Following this reasoning, for the literature review, different sources were analyzed for each 
methodology described. These resources varied from digital to printed and from academic courses to 
podcasts, with the intention to create a resourceful amount of data from different experts. For every 
case study, different people were involved in providing a different perspective to the semi-structured 
interview questionnaire. The same structure was used for all the cases while the responses were 
cross-examined assuring the information converged.  
All the interviewees in the case studies were provided with a brief explanation of the research 
objective and the type of questions that would be asked in each interaction. Although the informants 
in each case study were aware of the nature of the questions, they were ﬁrst presented during the 
interactions to promote a natural answer. This posed a risk to how informants will not answer 
expectedly and make the interview diverse in the information collected. This was noted by the 
researcher as in some cases the informants guided their answers into different topics, even when they 
were not asked to do so. Even so, it is complicated to identify if this happened due to cultural 
differences or the interviewer’s language could have provided a different perspective for understanding 
the questionnaire.  
In each study case description, the theoretical constructs of validity should be clear. All case study 
participants had the opportunity to review their answers and provide comments on their ﬁrst and last 
interactions, as these were structured as online surveys. The second interaction took the form of a 
semi constructed interview, where the interactions were recorded and transcripted, providing the 
opportunity to discuss and reﬂect upon each case with others. Moreover, all the informants had the 
chance to review and comment on their case data once this was transcripted and the survey results 
were done.  
8.1.2 Internal validity 
Internal validity describes the extent that the gathered evidence can support the studies. Since the 
foundation to prove internal validity is to establish a causal relationship (Trochim, 2020), the evidence 
gathered throughout the project is detailed. As mentioned in Chapter 3, for the Design Research 
methodology each phase of the research is needed in order to do the next one (see ﬁgure 29). The 
literature analysis in Chapter 2 provides the necessary information to construct the study case 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
54
==PAGE:56==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
questionnaires, focusing on their nature and design methods. Followed by this, the answers in the 
interviews offer the information needed to begin a conceptualization process (see chapters 5 and 6). 
Finally, the proposed tool is a result of the previous ﬁndings and by being evaluated by speciﬁc 
stakeholders, provides the validity of the tool (see chapter 7). 
 
Figure 29 - Internal validity model 
Moreover, the informants were asked to reﬂect on their answers in each interaction. In order to make 
these interactions as unbiased as possible, the perception survey remained anonymous. Again, this 
effect cannot be completely assured as the relationship between the informants and the researcher 
could have provoked an effect on their answers.  
8.1.3 External validity 
External validity relates to the way in which the gathered evidence can be applied outside the context 
of the studied case. Usually, case studies tend to be diminished due to their lack of generalisability. 
Even though the number of case studies for this project is considered poor for statistical purposes, 
they provide material for an analytical approach. Moreover, it is possible to apply the design research 
methodology in each case and use it to analyze each startup context. Therefore, it can be claimed a 
generalization of the theoretical framework for technology adoption models.  
The risks of arguing for external validation relies on the possibility that the ﬁndings might be only 
speciﬁc to the case studies. As mentioned in Chapter 3, the selection of case studies was not 
random as they follow speciﬁc criteria to be part of this research project; 1) impact on emerging 
markets, 2) operate from either in or outside the context to work with, 3) experience talking with 
customers and users, and 4) at least two iterations of prototypes. However, the number of team 
members and the type of technology was not a decisive factor but a characteristic of the maturity of 
each project. All case studies volunteered to be part of the research, leading to the belief that the 
startups intentions were aligned to the purpose of this project.  
The ﬁrst three case studies, ServedOnSalt, Nature Boards, and Ponik are selected as a result of their 
direct impact on sustainability in emerging markets. ServedOnSalt is the only study case in Denmark 
that already has established clients and investors network which gives the researcher a view of a 
proven development process. On the other hand, Nature Boards and Ponik provide the insights of a 
non-reﬂected process for developing innovation in an existing market. Also, all of the Danish cases 
represent a unique example of this research. Firstly, they all represent an early-stage startup (no more 
than two years after foundation). Secondly, they are all composed of two persons, providing the 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
55
==PAGE:57==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
characteristics of multitasking team members and horizontal decision making. Finally, as they have 
developed a new technology, they confront entering an existing market with disruptive innovation.  
The Mexican case studies, OS City, Cultivo, and Kabaan are selected as a result of their impact and 
closeness to their target market. Considering that these three case studies are part of the ecosystem 
to work with, they share characteristics that aided in arguing the external validity of the research. As a 
ﬁrst instance, they all have developed their technology to improve current systems. Although OS City 
focuses on digital systems, Cultivo and Kabaan have a more physical outcome when executed, 
making the user interact with the results faster. On the other hand, as OS City works with 
governmental strategies, the replicable characteristic is more feasible in different contexts due to 
political systems. In addition to this, the three cases have a generalization of common opportunity 
areas in emerging markets (Government, Sustainability and Technology).  
8.1.4 Reliability 
Reliability relates to the degree to which the experiments can produce similar results in different 
contexts. However, the use of Design Research implies that the case studies will not be found in the 
same status at any other point, this due to the fast pace they work and the learning it comes with. 
Although, Checkland and Holwell (1998) highlight the principle of recoverability rather than 
repeatability in research. This means that anyone interested in the research can be able to recover the 
process and trace its method, and therefore, understand where the ﬁndings come from.  
Chapter 3 describes the process in an intended way towards transparency for all readers. As such, 
the appendixes present all the information gathered but personal notes and conversations, if 
requested, the researcher would share this information. 
8.2 Findings in relation to existing knowledge 
In this section, the ﬁndings in relation to domestication processes in startups to the existing 
knowledge will be described. To discuss these concerns, the ﬁndings have been listed as the order of 
the research questions. 1) Understanding of domestication models  , 2) Conceptualization of 
domestication models, and 3) Development of domestication tools. 
The ﬁndings are considered causal as explained in the internal validity section. However, each section 
is considered to be distinct ﬁndings due to the adaptability of knowledge in different settings. It is not 
only assumed that the ﬁndings of this research can lead to speciﬁc input for future research but also 
can contribute as general knowledge of domestication processes in startups. 
8.2.1 Understanding of domestication models 
Concerning the ﬁrst research question, this project had three main ﬁndings through literature review 
and empirical data about existing knowledge. As a ﬁrst, the understanding of models by startups 
relies uniquely on upon as a communication tool inside the operating system rather than a process to 
follow. The second ﬁnding relates to the adopted meaning of user experience instead of the 
domestication process. And thirdly, hyper-collaborations activities are recognized as a way to enrich 
product development but by being an open-source startups feel a risk of becoming copied by 
someone who has more resources and has a better positioning in the market. 
Along with the literature review for social shaping theories, researchers discuss the importance of 
understanding how customers interact with products throughout life cycles (Buttle, 2009). A 
continuous point of these theories was the low reliability of the data due to the focus on individual 
perceptions as opposed to group perceptions. This results, in a lack of quantitative data, to make 
decisions based on facts (Pikkarainen 2004). The ﬁrst ﬁnding of this thesis was that startups consider 
models and frameworks as an internal language to identify their processes, rather than as guidance. 
This is because startups believe using models solely based on questionable values produces a limited 
predictive power to potential investors. To support this, the limited power perspective was mentioned 
by Chuttur in relation to the Technology Acceptance Model (Chuttur 2009).   
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
56
==PAGE:58==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
The second ﬁnding related to the understanding of technology adoption models correlates to the 
identiﬁed term of user experience as an adoption process. Although both terms share similar 
characteristics, adoption models look for a balance between technology and user, while user 
experience focuses on the development of products based on the user’s interests (Marcus, 2019). 
Furthermore, the case studies have a demographic of 23 to 32 years old between all the informants, 
meaning they tend to learn from recent models that are better positioned in digital resources. The only 
member above 30 years old was the only informant that mentioned resources from 1980 in 
comparison to the other ones. Referencing Kotsch, the ideal age of a startup founder is between 30 
and 50 due to their experience (Kotsch, 2017). For this speciﬁc case, this can be validated as this 
startup is the largest of the case studies in relation to team members, impact, and income.  
The third ﬁnding details that startups are aware of the potential of using hyper collaboration strategies 
to enrich their product development and adoption potential (Kolk, Eagar, Boulton, & Mira, 2017). 
However, their context and past relationships play a key role in implementing this decision (Bryan, 
2017). Danish startups shared a mutual interest in sharing their information with relevant stakeholders, 
while Mexican startups believe sharing their information will result in the creation of a new competitor. 
Based on Ordoñez’s research, it was considered that these beliefs are related to the economic and 
political context each startup has overcome (Ordoñez, 2013). 
8.2.2 Conceptualisation of domestication models 
Concerning the second research question, a domestication tool was developed so startups can 
quickly and easily deﬁne and communicate to which extend a technology meets the adoption process 
factors. The proposed tool was based on two main ﬁndings uncovered in the conceptualization 
process.  
The ﬁrst ﬁnding for this research question was the use of tools rather than models in the startup 
ecosystem. While some research articles emphasize the need for fast pace routines in startups 
(Bhargava, 2020), others contrast this point and cite the importance of a constant pace within the 
startup environment (Harlan, 2017). The case studies highlighted a lack of interest in applying models 
to their development processes but an interest in learning them. This ﬁnding came from two main 
quotes that then were validated by other informants, “We absorb the methodology rather than follow 
it” (Appendix 8 - Transcript Kabaan Interview) and “Methodologies are part of our DNA” (Appendix 9 - 
Transcript Cultivo Land PBC Interview). Additionally, when inquiring about the reasoning for their 
arguments, the informants mentioned the amount of time needed to follow a model was a barrier, 
especially when they were not certain if the model was appropriate for their business model. Keeping 
this in mind, this thesis prioritized the development of a tool that could provide an iterative process 
without a considerable time investment.  
The second ﬁnding in the conceptualization process was the need for a visual tool for feasible 
improvement and consistent pace. It was discovered that startups tend to divide tasks and create 
agendas for teamwork moments, however, members will be in constant, short discussions due to the 
relation between tasks and the rapid work pace needed (Bhargava, 2020). Along the morphological 
chart process, it became a priority to understand the current state of the given startup and identify 
areas of opportunity and risks. 
The proposed tool is similar to the customer journey map proposed by IDEO in 1999 (Stephanidis, 
2014), however, instead of attempting to create strategies for each touchpoint, the tool visualizes the 
technology features as the main component to entering an emerging market. As such, the tool is a 
graphical representation likewise IDEO and Strategyzer have done in the past, positioning iterative and 
graphical tools into the startup ecosystem (AG, n.d.) (IDEO,n.d.) 
8.2.3 Development of domestication tools 
To answer the third research question, a tool was introduced and evaluated by the case studies. Each 
study case received an interactive board and a perceived value survey to evaluate the development of 
the tool in the internal startup ecosystem (Chapter 7). 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
57
==PAGE:59==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
Although the survey consisted of qualitative and quantitative data, the main ﬁndings for this research 
question came from the open-ended questions. The ﬁrst ﬁnding was that the tool provided a window 
to ﬁnd discrepancies of product views among the teams (i.e. compare Head of Product vs Head of 
Sales canvas), making sure that the features in the product create value for the user. As mentioned by 
the case studies, the tool worked as a centralized command for the activities related to product 
development to sale channels, without the need of discussing each feature. As stated by Willink's 
research, that has led enormous companies to take action plans and make them a success story, 
action rather than the discussion is crucial (Willink, 2017).  
Moreover, the second ﬁnding was that the tool ﬁts in the opportunity to move from early adopters to 
early majority, resulting in a growth opportunity for the company. As Moore mentions, the chasm is the 
point where cutting-edge products can progressively enter larger markets (Moore, 2001). It was 
determined that startups can use this tool to cultivate strategies based on the customer categories 
identiﬁed by Easely (2010) and provoke a better adoption of the product in their target market.  
 
Figure 30 - The Chasm, Moore 2001 
8.3 Other analysis for Domestication Models 
This thesis focuses on the Engineering Design perspective of domestication models in startups. This 
project is suitable for startups and industry experts as this understanding of design and research are 
most widely used. Nevertheless, if the author had based the research paradigm, different 
interpretations could have been made. 
During the process, the researcher had a big amount of contextual insights, while at the same time, 
the aim of the project objective was the main focus. Determinants such as cultural factors, political 
circumstances, gender behavior, and contextual forces were ﬁltered out as they were not fundamental 
factors for this research. It could be interesting to complement this research with social studies, as it 
might result in a different interpretation of the ﬁnding or help validate them.   
8.4 Summary 
In this chapter, the main ﬁndings of the project are discussed. Following the methodology described in 
Chapter 3, the validity of the research was argued with the following arguments: 
•
Construct validity  is assured with various types of sources creating a chain of evidence 
throughout the project. For the literature review, different sources provided space for a broader 
analysis. Additionally, various individuals were involved in the reviewing of the study case 
guidelines to provide a diverse perspective in the empirical data. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
58
==PAGE:60==
Discussion                                                                                                                                                               Chapter 8
•
Internal validity is guaranteed by presenting the causal relation between the gathered data in 
the project. Through a model, it was exhibited how the information was necessary to achieve 
all the phases of the selected methodology in Chapter 3. 
•
The external validity of this project is not ensured completely. Even though the selected case 
studies followed speciﬁc criteria, due to the time-maturity factor, it will be complicated to use 
the same case studies for future research.  
•
The reliability factor is based on the transparency of the researcher's work and the availability 
of data on this project.  
Furthermore, the ﬁndings of the research are discussed concerning the existing knowledge in the 
ﬁeld. 
•
The understanding of domestication models research comes with similar conclusions to 
existing ﬁndings. The ﬁrst ﬁnding is how startups consider models as an internal language to 
identify their processes, rather than guidance. Secondly, the understanding of technology 
adoption models is inclined to the term of user experience as an adoption process. Finally, 
context and past relationships play a key role in implementing hyper-collaboration strategies. 
•
The appropriate approach for the conceptualization of domestication models comes with two 
main ﬁndings. First, startups prefer the use of tools rather than models due to the amount of 
time that has to be invested. As the second ﬁnding, there is a need to understand the current 
state of startups and identify opportunity areas that could be taken one by one. 
•
The tool development identiﬁes two ﬁndings. First, the tool provides a window to ﬁnd 
discrepancies of product views among the teams and assure value for the customer. Second, 
this is an opportunity to ensure a path from early adopters to early majority, making it a growth 
opportunity for the company. 
 
To conclude the chapter, external factors of the research were mentioned as an alternative 
perspective to create a broader knowledge of the research results. 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities

==PAGE:61==
Conclusion                                                                                                                                                              Chapter 9
 9. Conclusion 
This master thesis has researched the understanding, conceptualization and development of 
domestication models in the startup ecosystem. This chapter reviews the main ﬁndings concerning 
the research questions and recognizes the core contributions to the domestication ﬁeld. 
9.1 Research ﬁndings 
Three study areas and six case studies located in two countries provided insights and evidence for 
answering the research questions. To address the concluding remarks for this project, each question 
will be answered in the research order.  
Q1: How are the current domestication models understood? 
Q2: How may a domestication model be conceptualized? 
Q3: How may a domestication model be developed in Startups? 
These questions guided the process for establishing the theoretical foundation and identifying the 
focal areas for the case studies. A combination of literature review and empirical data is applied to 
provide reliable frameworks for each research question.  
9.1.1 Q1: How are the current domestication models understood? 
Social shaping models are theories that show how users come to accept and use technology in their 
daily life. These theories focus on how each person has a choice on product use and therefore, how it 
will affect the trajectory of innovation in that area. Domestication models, in comparison, are focused 
on the balance between technical features and customer needs. In this way, both supply and demand 
needs are represented in a mediation process. 
The research in this project shows three dimensions of the domestication process: (1) Technological 
Innovation, (2) Social Shaping and (3) Startup Business. Following the research ﬁndings, six attributes 
are considered as the support of the domestication process models. 
  
• Business strategy - Startups rely on their value proposition to be economically sustainable.  
• Technological potential - The degree in which the solution can produce similar results in different 
contexts. 
• Customer activity focus -  Value and fulﬁllment of needs can only be understood by analyzing 
customer and user behavior with and without the solution. 
• System Thinking - By realizing systems are made of different factors that will affect the perspective 
of each technology.  
• Life cycle consideration - Considering the production and consumption of the solution in a different 
context. 
• Network potential - Understanding the potential of different stakeholders in different industries. 
The case studies show the relationship between each attribute and the effect it has on the decision-
making process. These attributes are fundamental to understand how a domestication tool might be 
conceptualized, as it is recognized that the base for a domestication model is a system dynamic 
behavior.  
9.1.2 Q2: How may a domestication model be conceptualised? 
Domestication models focus on the characteristics that inﬂuence the relationship between the 
customer and the technology. The conceptualization process presents the modeling of the solution 
according to the previously stated attributes. These perspectives support the development of the 
conceptualization process through paradoxes, creating categories for the literature and study case 
ﬁndings. 
 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
60
==PAGE:62==
Conclusion                                                                                                                                                              Chapter 9
Further, in the conceptualization process, the ﬁndings are set up a in morphology chart, manifesting 
the essential characteristics that the domestication model should have. Here are the characteristics 
that are identiﬁed as strategic to conceptualize the proposed tool: 
• Creation of a tangible representation of the current state. 
• Ideas can be created, validated, and ﬁxed at any point. 
• Promote feasible improvement and consistent pace. 
• Find the balance between client perception and company values. 
• Help identify opportunities and risks. 
Even though this tool creates a respectable approach for a domestication tool that startups can 
adopt, it was not tested in a real-life scenario.  
9.1.3 Q3: How may a domestication model be developed in Startups? 
The development of a domestication model in the context of a startup is more than just the task of 
adopting a new framework into the daily activities. Rather, developing a domestication model needs to 
consider team dynamics and patterns of product planning to create the most beneﬁts. For a 
domestication model in the startup context, the main assumption is that the tool or model can 
capitalize the products Minimus Viable Product and create an offer that will make the startup stand 
out from competitors in the market.  
The proposed tool is introduced to the case studies to validate its development potential within the 
startup context. Based on the empirical data, the biggest challenges to adopt the tool are identifying 
the right product features, reﬂecting on the current state of the startup, and deﬁning the customer 
preferences. On the other hand, the case studies perceive the tool as a meaningful information 
resource and a way to evaluate the beneﬁt of the product features.  
Considering these ﬁndings, to develop a domestication model in a startup, this should be concise and 
focused on one thing (i.e, product improvement), provide clarity to any member who views the results, 
and allow making changes at any point.  
9.2 Core contributions 
As this master thesis seeks to develop more knowledge in domestication models in startups, it seems 
logical to list the main contributions following the objectives of this project. 
• An exploration of the domestication processes between startups that are part of their impact 
context and the ones that operate from a different one. 
• The identiﬁcation of attributes of Domestication models for a systematic development; Business 
strategy, Technological potential, Customer activity focus, System Thinking, Life cycle consideration 
and Network potential.  
• The mapping of domestication characteristics in a conceptualization process. 
• A visual tool that allows startups to ensure their path from early adopters to early majority based on 
their technology features.  
Finally, this project contributes to the current product development processes and the customer 
validation strategies.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
61
==PAGE:63==
Perspectives                                                                                                                                                         Chapter 10
10. Perspectives 
In this chapter, the objective set for this thesis, To develop a domestication tool that can be 
applied in daily activities of startups, is reﬂected upon in relation to the research process and main 
ﬁndings. Moreover, the chapter considers the areas where it can be involved in and ﬁndings worth 
exploring for deeper research. 
10.1 Reﬂections on the project 
This project identiﬁes opportunities related to how startups can improve their positioning in the 
market, moving towards a more customer conscious way. Still, it is understandable that in real life, the 
adoption of this tool comes with challenges and limits for implementing in a daily routine. Moreover, 
due to limited time, veriﬁcation of the tool was not carried out and a value perception analysis was 
made. It is acknowledged that the results of this project are not absolute, but they do lead to new 
research in different areas.  
10.1.1 Industry 
Understanding the domestication process represents a fundamental opportunity for startups. Even 
though there is a need to verify the credibility of the proposed tool, this thesis gives a set of design 
attributes; Business strategy, Technological potential, Customer activity focus, System Thinking, Life 
cycle consideration, and Network potential. In addition to these, a morphological chart that can be 
applied for any startup needs in the domestication process. These ﬁndings can also be relevant to 
study how product development affects business development.  
10.1.2 Research 
This project considers the most recognized theories and models about the domestication process 
from three different study areas to provide a systematic understanding and development of a new 
tool. The identiﬁcation of these study areas provides insights regarding the relevance of the balance 
between customer and company.  
The proposed tool can be used as a guide to understand the relationship between customer and the 
technology from a domestication perspective. If the tool was used by a sample group, this could 
provide empirical data to validate the relevance in the startup ecosystem. 
Besides, this thesis extends the knowledge regarding the startups needs for positioning in new 
markets. Future research in this area will support in understanding the evolution of needs in each 
development stage. 
10.1.3 Education 
Social Shaping of Technology theories are already part of Design and Innovation programs in different 
academic organizations. However, these theories should be applied in real life scenarios to create the 
necessary skills to shape conscious engineers. Domestication is about ﬁnding the balance between 
customer-company, focusing in understanding the role that technology has in daily life. Products are 
not only problem-solving artifacts, they represent a small part of the user, and as engineers it should 
be conscious of how they form people with what we develop.   
10.2 Suggestions for future work 
As before mentioned, this thesis is a step towards new research either in the engineering ﬁeld or 
social studies. This project provided meaningful insights about innovation processes in startups and 
has helped build a conceptual tool in the ﬁeld. To create different perspectives on how startups can 
adopt domestication strategies, more research is required to support these conclusions. A list of 
possible future work suggestion is presented: 
•
A domestication model to approach the relationship between customer and company needs - 
a methodological approach for product development considering the domestication factors 
and customer and company characteristics.  
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities 
 
62
==PAGE:64==
Perspectives                                                                                                                                                         Chapter 10
•
Collaboration with early stage startups to validate the proposal tool - capturing empirical data 
that can be actively monitored in the development of the proposed tool. 
•
Management of consumer needs - considering a domestication approach, research how 
consumers can increase their loyalty to the brand while reducing their consumption. 
•
Evolution of needs in startups development stages - capturing empirical data from past and 
current stages of startups and monitor their identiﬁcation of needs as an internal and external 
perspective.

12. Appendices 
Appendix 1 - 1st Interaction with Case studies Questionnaire 
1st Questionnaire guideline for case studies in the thesis project  
”Domestication of Technology: Innovations making sense within communities” 
Researcher: Martha Cecilia Fernandez Contreras 
Technical University of Denmark 
Contact: ceciliafdz.94@gmail.com  
This interview guideline has two parts: 
• Demographics of Startup - These questions attempt to identify characteristics as foundation, 
location, team member characteristics 
• Use of methods and reﬂection - These questions focus in the identiﬁcation and use of methods in 
daily activities  
Demographics of Startup  
• Open-ended question: What is the name of the Startup? 
• Open-ended question: Where are you located? 
• Open-ended question: How many people operate in the Startup? 
• Option question: Does the team members have different nationalities? 
• Option question: Does the team members have different study lines? 
• Open-ended question: In which year did the operation of the Startup begun? (if started in a course, 
write down the year you took it) 
• Option question: Which Sustainable Development Goal do you target? 
• Option question: Check all the programs or activities you have been part of 
Use of methods 
• Option question: Have you use any of the following design methodologies or method? 
• Scale question: How many times did you or have you modiﬁed your prototype 
• Open-ended question: How did you get insights from your user? 
Domesticating technology: Innovations making sense within communities
